        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.1c14980"><meta property="og:title" content="Long-Term Stable, High-Capacity Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Taking a Closer Look at CrPS4 from an Electrochemical and Mechanistic Point of View"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/acsami.1c14980.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Electrochemical performance of the layered compound CrPS4 for the usage as anode material in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) was examined and exceptional reversible long-term capacity and capacity retention were found. After 300 cycles, an extraordinary reversible capacity of 687 mAh g–1 at a current rate of 1 A g–1 was achieved, while rate capability tests showed an excellent capacity retention of 100%. Detailed evaluation of the data evidence a change of the electrochemical reaction upon cycling leading to the striking long-term performance. Further investigations targeted the reaction mechanism of the first cycle by applying complementary techniques, i.e., powder X-ray diffraction (XRD), pair distribution function (PDF) analysis, X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS), and 23Na/31P magic-angle-spinning (MAS) nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy. The results indicated an unexpectedly complex reaction pathway including formation of several intercalation compounds, depending on the amount of Na inserted at the early discharge states and subsequent conversion to Na2S and strongly disordered metallic Cr at the completely discharged state. While XAS measurements suggest no further presence of intermediates after formation of Na intercalation compounds, several different phases are detected via MAS NMR upon continued discharging. Especially the data obtained from the MAS NMR investigations therefore point toward a very complex reaction pathway. Furthermore, solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation, resulting in the presence of NaF, was observed. After recharging the anode material, no structural long-range order occurred, but short-range order indeed resembled the local environment of the starting material, to a certain extent."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/acsami.1c14980.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6e45c8ac5775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e45c8ac5775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '5a70870e-de0a-4a3d-a738-5c8602f8ff77'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e45c8ac5775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Long-Term Stable, High-Capacity Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Taking a Closer Look at CrPS&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; from an Electrochemical and Mechanistic Point of View", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c14980", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Long-Term Stable, High-Capacity Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Taking a Closer Look at CrPS&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; from an Electrochemical and Mechanistic Point of View", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c14980", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Long-Term Stable, High-Capacity Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Taking a Closer Look at CrPS&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; from an Electrochemical and Mechanistic Point of View", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c14980", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Long-Term Stable, High-Capacity Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Taking a Closer Look at CrPS&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; from an Electrochemical and Mechanistic Point of View", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c14980", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {

                    }, 1000);
                }
            }

                articleAbstractCCHover = true;

        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {

            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {

class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D54936%26pageCount%3D15%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJonas%2Bvan%2BDinter%252C%2BSylvio%2BIndris%252C%2BAlexander%2BBitter%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D46%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c14980%26title%3DLong-Term%2BStable%252C%2BHigh-Capacity%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BTaking%2Ba%2BCloser%2BLook%2Bat%2BCrPS4%2Bfrom%2Ban%2BElectrochemical%2Band%2BMechanistic%2BPoint%2Bof%2BView%26numPages%3D15%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D54950%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2021" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_0012.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0012.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14980&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Electrochemical performance of the layered compound CrPS<sub>4</sub> for the usage as anode material in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) was examined and exceptional reversible long-term capacity and capacity retention were found. After 300 cycles, an extraordinary reversible capacity of 687 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current rate of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> was achieved, while rate capability tests showed an excellent capacity retention of 100%. Detailed evaluation of the data evidence a change of the electrochemical reaction upon cycling leading to the striking long-term performance. Further investigations targeted the reaction mechanism of the first cycle by applying complementary techniques, i.e., powder X-ray diffraction (XRD), pair distribution function (PDF) analysis, X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS), and <sup>23</sup>Na/<sup>31</sup>P magic-angle-spinning (MAS) nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy. The results indicated an unexpectedly complex reaction pathway including formation of several intercalation compounds, depending on the amount of Na inserted at the early discharge states and subsequent conversion to Na<sub>2</sub>S and strongly disordered metallic Cr at the completely discharged state. While XAS measurements suggest no further presence of intermediates after formation of Na intercalation compounds, several different phases are detected via MAS NMR upon continued discharging. Especially the data obtained from the MAS NMR investigations therefore point toward a very complex reaction pathway. Furthermore, solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation, resulting in the presence of NaF, was observed. After recharging the anode material, no structural long-range order occurred, but short-range order indeed resembled the local environment of the starting material, to a certain extent.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a NMR</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">To reach the targeted goal to limit global warming, which was confirmed by most states worldwide in the Paris Agreement, a shift from fossil energy sources toward clean alternative technologies using wind, solar, tidal, or geothermal energy is strongly required. As a part of this transition, suitable energy storage systems must be identified and developed to compensate the natural fluctuation of electrical energy output from sustainable technologies. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> This topic is already discussed in the literature and it remains questionable if the established Li<sup>+</sup> ion batteries (LIBs) are suitable for this purpose. The alternative are systems based on Na, because of its similar chemical properties and the high abundance worldwide. Nonetheless, Na has a higher atomic weight, as well as a larger ionic radius and a lower chemical potential. However, these properties are no major drawbacks, as the total weight of the device is not as important for stationary energy storage as for mobile applications, where a low weight and high energy density is necessary or beneficial. In battery science, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been in focus for several years and one of the biggest challenges is the development of anode materials that provide high specific capacities, long-term stability consisting of inexpensive, earth-abundant, and sustainable elements. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> To ensure high specific capacities, materials undergoing conversion instead of intercalation reactions are preferred, where the constituents of the active material are completely reduced enabling uptake and release of larger amounts of alkali-metal ions per formula unit (f.u.). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Widely investigated conversion materials are, e.g., binary <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 5 to 13">(5−13)</a> and, less often, ternary <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 14 to 16">(14−16)</a> metal chalcogenides, as well as alloys <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a> and other metal salts such as (fluoro)phosphates or polyanionic compounds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> Many of these compounds contain elements such as Cu, Ni, and/or Co, which are also key ingredients of LIBs, but in terms of sustainability, availability, and price, these elements are quite problematic. Choosing elements that are less problematic and/or developing efficient battery recycling concepts is a prerequisite for producing inexpensive and sustainable electrode materials in the long term. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Regarding the anodes for SIBs, different sulfides were studied intensely and are considered promising active materials in SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref15 ref16 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref15 ref16 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 12, 15, 16 and 21 to 25">(12,15,16,21−25)</a> The M–S bond is less polar than a M–O bond, which is favorable for conversion reactions in SIBs, because lower ionicity leads to decreasing overpotentials during charging and discharging. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> However, interesting active materials such as transition-metal orthothiophosphates (M<sub><i>x</i></sub>PS<sub>4</sub>) are less often studied, although the presence of phosphorus might be beneficial, because of the possibility to form alloys with sodium, providing higher specific capacities. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref27" aria-label="reference 18 and 27">(18,27)</a> Orthothiophosphates M<sub><i>x</i></sub>PS<sub>4</sub> (M<sup>III</sup> = Cr, B, Al, Ga, In, Bi; M<sup>I</sup> = Li, Na, Ag, Cu; M<sup>II</sup> = Pd) exhibit various dimensionalities and contain transition or alkali metals, as well as <i>p</i>-block elements with oxidation numbers ranging from 1 to 3. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 28 to 38">(28−38)</a> The layered compound CrPS<sub>4</sub> has been thoroughly investigated since its discovery in 1976. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> The magnetic properties, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 39 to 41">(39−41)</a> optical behavior, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref42" aria-label="reference 39 and 42">(39,42)</a> and chemical compositions and oxidation states, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> spin dynamics, and electronic and thermal transport properties <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 41, 44 and 45">(41,44,45)</a> were studied. Applying different exfoliation techniques generates few layer CrPS<sub>4</sub> materials, which are mainly studied with respect to changes of the properties, depending on the thickness and/or number of layers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 46 to 48">(46−48)</a> Applications in energy storage and conversion such as catalysis <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> and as intercalation electrodes in LIBs were investigated. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Although CrPS<sub>4</sub> is known to intercalate up to 2 Li/f.u. using butyl-lithium, electrochemical intercalation shows rather poor intercalation behavior with only &lt;1 Li/f.u. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> However, as far as we know, CrPS<sub>4</sub> has never been investigated for the application in SIBs until now. In addition, chromium is not a typical main element of battery technology but has a slightly higher abundance than Ni, Co, or Cu, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> which makes it a possible substitution for these elements. Although electrochemical intercalation of Li into CrPS<sub>4</sub> exhibits very low degrees of intercalation, the application in SIBs is promising, if a conversion mechanism occurs during charging and discharging. Since sodium and phosphorus can form alloys, a conversion-alloying mechanism also seems reasonable. For a conversion reaction with subsequent alloying (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>), the theoretically very high specific capacity is calculated to be 1396 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>.<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i3"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>A conversion reaction without subsequent alloying theoretically delivers a specific capacity of 1015 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and follows <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i4"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>Both of these possible reaction pathways provide very high specific capacities in SIBs, which is beneficial for future applications. Therefore, we investigated the electrochemical behavior of crystalline CrPS<sub>4</sub> as anode material in SIBs and applied a variety of methods including X-ray diffraction (XRD) and X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS), and pair-distribution function analyses (PDF), as well as <sup>31</sup>P/<sup>23</sup>Na magic-angle-spinning (MAS) nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy to elucidate the reaction mechanism during the sodiation process.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.1.  Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">CrPS<sub>4</sub> was synthesized via chemical vapor transport. Stoichiometric amounts of chromium (Alfa Aesar, 99.94%), red phosphorus (Knapsack AG, 99.9999%), and excess sulfur (Chempur, 99.999%) were heated in a quartz glass ampule, which was previously sealed under vacuum (&lt;10<sup>–4</sup> mbar). The ampule was placed in a tube furnace and heated to 723 K within 4 h. This temperature was maintained for 24 h and afterward increased to 973 K within 2.5 h. After this temperature was reached, a temperature gradient to 953 K was established within the quartz glass ampule to start the transport reaction. Excess sulfur was utilized as transport agent and the transport reaction was performed for 2 weeks.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.2.  Electrochemical Tests</h3><div class="NLM_p last">70 wt % of CrPS<sub>4</sub>, 20 wt % SUPER C65 (Timcal, Switzerland), and 10 wt % polyvinylidene difluoride (PVDF, Solvay, Germany) were mixed and subsequently suspended in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP, Fisher Bioreagens, 99.8%) for electrode preparation. The obtained slurry was spread on Cu foil applying the doctor-blade casting method. Afterward, the electrode film was dried overnight at room temperature and for several hours in a vacuum oven at 333 K. For test cell assembly, circular electrodes with a diameter of 10 mm (0.88–1.34 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> active material) were punched out of the film. The electrochemical reaction was conducted in Swagelok-type cells, which were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox (99.999% Ar, MBraun Unilab, &lt;1 ppm of H<sub>2</sub>O; &lt;1 ppm of O<sub>2</sub>). Glass fiber filters (Whatman, United Kingdom) and Celgard membranes were used as separators. Sodium metal foil was used as a counter electrode and a 1 M solution of NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> (abcr, 98%) in bis(2-methoxyethyl)ether (Diglyme, Acros Organics, 99+%, extra dry) was used as an electrolyte. Galvanostatic cycling within the potential range of 3.0–0.1 V was performed on Neware 8 channel battery analyzers. For battery performance tests, a current rate of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the first and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for subsequent cycles were used. For rate capability tests, current rates of 0.1, 0.2. 0.5, 1.0, 1.5, 2.0, and 3.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> were applied. Constant current–constant voltage (CCCV) charge mode was applied for all cycles with current rates of ≥0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The final current rate of the CCCV mode was set to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the long-term stability tests and to 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the rate capability measurements. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were performed on a Zahner XPOT within the same voltage range as mentioned above and at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.3.  Material Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Powder XRD was conducted using Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.54058 Å) with a PANalytical diffractometer equipped with a 1D PIXcel detector. SEM and EDX experiments were performed on a Philips ESEM XL 30 system that was equipped with an EDAX New XL-30 detector. Raman spectra were recorded at RT on a Bruker Vertex70 RAM II FT-Raman spectrometer using an Nd:YAG laser with an excitation wavelength of λ = 1064 nm and a liquid-nitrogen-cooled, highly sensitive Ge detector with a resolution of 3 cm<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">2.4.  Investigation of the Mechanism by XRD, PDF, XAS, and <sup>23</sup>Na/<sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR</h3><div class="NLM_p">For the investigation of the reaction mechanism, samples were prepared by pressing 70 wt % CrPS<sub>4</sub> and 30 wt % SUPER C65 into pellets and assembling them in Swagelok-type cells. The electrochemical reaction was interrupted after uptake of a distinct amount of sodium (discharge) and afterward the Swagelok cells were disassembled in an argon glovebox. To remove the electrolyte, electrodes were rinsed with pure diglyme and dried for 1 day. These air-sensitive samples were permanently handled and transported in an inert atmosphere.</div><div class="NLM_p">XRD and PDF experiments were performed in Debye–Scherrer geometry using high-energy synchrotron radiation at beamline P02.1, Petra III (DESY, Hamburg) at a wavelength of λ = 0.207 Å (60 keV). Samples were prepared in borosilicate capillaries with a diameter of 0.7 mm. The sample-to-detector distance (SDD) for XRD experiments was 1010 mm and 350 mm for PDF experiments. The detector used was a PerkinElmer XRD1621 2D detector. The obtained 2D data were processed using the software DAWN Science, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> and xPDFsuite <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> was used to obtain the PDF (<i>Q</i><sub>max</sub> = 19 Å<sup>–1</sup>). To account for instrumental contributions to XRD, a LaB<sub>6</sub> (NIST 660b) standard was measured. An empty capillary was measured under identical conditions to subtract the contribution of the glass capillary from the PDF.</div><div class="NLM_p">XAS experiments on the Cr K-edge were performed at the beamline B18, Diamond Light Source (U.K.). The samples were diluted with carbon, pressed into pellets with a diameter of 8 mm, and sealed between Kapton foil. Each sample was measured three times at the minimum with a collection time of 3 min and these spectra were merged. For energy calibration of the raw data, Cr foil was used. To ensure appropriate normalization and background subtraction, the pre-edge region was described by a linear fit, and for the post-edge region, a cubic polynomial fit was applied. XAFS data reduction was performed by using a calculated background from the Autobk algorithm of the program Athena. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> A fitting range in <i>k</i>-space between 3.0 Å<sup>–1</sup> and 12.5 Å<sup>–1</sup> was chosen by applying <i>k</i><sup>3</sup> weighting. The <i>k</i><sup>3</sup>-weighted χ(<i>k</i>) function was then multiplied by a Kaiser–Bessel window and Fourier-transformed into <i>R</i>-space in order to obtain the pseudoradial distribution function (pRDF) |χ(<i>R</i>)| without correcting the distances by a phase shift.</div><div class="NLM_p last"><sup>23</sup>Na and <sup>31</sup>P magic-angle spinning (MAS) nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy was performed on a Bruker Avance neo 200 MHz spectrometer at a magnetic field of 4.7 T, corresponding to resonance frequencies of 52.9 MHz (<sup>23</sup>Na) and 81.0 MHz (<sup>31</sup>P). Spinning was done in 1.3 mm rotors at 60 kHz. Spectra were acquired with a rotor-synchronized Hahn-echo pulse sequence, a recycle delay of 1 s, and π/2 pulse durations of 1.3 μs (<sup>23</sup>Na) and 0.95 μs (<sup>31</sup>P). The NMR shifts of <sup>23</sup>Na and <sup>31</sup>P were referenced to an aqueous 1 M NaCl solution and to H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (85%), respectively.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">3.1.  Characterization of Pristine CrPS<sub>4</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p">CrPS<sub>4</sub> crystallizes in the monoclinic noncentrosymmetric space group <i>C</i>2. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref39 ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref39 ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 28, 39, 41 and 42">(28,39,41,42)</a> The crystal structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a) is based on a hexagonal close packing of puckered sulfur layers. The trivalent Cr<sup>3+</sup> ions are located in a distorted octahedral environment of S<sup>2–</sup> anions, and P<sup>5+</sup> is arranged in a distorted tetrahedral coordination of S<sup>2–</sup>. The CrS<sub>6</sub> octahedra are connected via common edges forming chains, which are linked by the PS<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra to layers along the [001] direction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref39 ref42 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref39 ref42 ref44" aria-label="reference 28, 39, 42 and 44">(28,39,42,44)</a> The XRD pattern (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b) of the pristine material, compared to a calculated pattern using data from the literature, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> reveals that phase-pure and crystalline CrPS<sub>4</sub> was obtained.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Crystal structure of CrPS<sub>4</sub> along the <i>b</i>-axis. Blue octahedra indicate Cr<sup>3+</sup>, purple tetrahedra P<sup>5+</sup> coordination and yellow spheres illustrate the sulfur lattice. The structure was plotted using the software VESTA 3.4.0 <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> and crystallographic data from the literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> (b) XRD pattern of CrPS<sub>4</sub> compared to a pattern calculated from literature data. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a></p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14980&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Raman spectroscopy also confirms the presence of CrPS<sub>4</sub> (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> in the Supporting Information) and EDX measurements (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> in the Supporting Information) demonstrate ratios of Cr, P, and S close to the expected values, yielding the composition Cr<sub>0.98</sub>P<sub>1.06</sub>S<sub>3.95</sub>. In addition, SEM investigations (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> in the Supporting Information) confirm the layered nature of the compound.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">3.2.  Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">For galvanostatic long-term cycling, a current rate of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the first cycle and a rate of 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> for subsequent cycles were applied (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a). The capacity of the first discharge of 1226 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> corresponds to ∼9.7 Na/f.u., while the first charge process only yields 722 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼5.7 Na/f.u.). The discharge and charge capacities of the second cycle are significantly lower at 486 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼3.8 Na/f.u.) and 460 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼3.6 Na/f.u.), respectively. Nevertheless, starting from the third cycle, the Coulombic efficiency reached ∼100% and within the following 130 cycles, the discharge capacity rises to 737 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼5.8 Na/f.u.) and afterward drops again to 665 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼5.2 Na/f.u.) in the 165th cycle. After passing this minimum, the capacity recovers, increasing to 735 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼5.8 Na/f.u.) in the 206th cycle, followed by a slight but steady decrease, reaching a reversible specific capacity of 687 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼5.4 Na/f.u.) in the 300th cycle. The initial decrease and subsequent recovery of the specific capacity was already observed for several electrode materials in SIBs and was explained by different phenomena. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref21 ref25 ref53 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref21 ref25 ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 15, 21, 25, 53 and 54">(15,21,25,53,54)</a> In most cases, the underlying reaction mechanism during long-term cycling changes and the capacity recovery occurs over a large number of cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref25 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref25 ref53" aria-label="reference 15, 25 and 53">(15,25,53)</a> For the Na-CuS system, a relatively fast recovery of the initial capacity loss was observed, which was explained by a combination of synergistic effects, i.e., the formation of semicoherent phase interfaces arising from multiphase transitions causing stress relaxation and preventing random pulverization. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Because a similar behavior is found for CrPS<sub>4</sub>, at least comparable effects may play a key role. The evolution of the discharge and charge profiles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>b–d) clearly indicates changes of the reaction mechanism during long-term cycling. The initial discharge profile (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, inset) shows a potential decrease from 3.0 V to 1.65 V, where an electrochemical plateau is developed, typical for a two-phase reaction caused by intercalation of Na into the empty layers of CrPS<sub>4</sub>. Further Na uptake leads to a steep decrease of the potential until ∼0.98 V is reached, followed by a steady decrease, finally reaching a discharge capacity of 1226 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼9.7 Na/f.u.). The potential of the first charge profile quickly rises from 0.1 V to 1.98 V, where a pseudoplateau is developed. Afterward, the potential rises again until the next pseudoplateau occurs at 2.51 V. Further charging exhibits a potential increase delivering an overall charge capacity of 722 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼5.7 Na/f.u.). Considering the possible reaction pathways represented by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eqs 1">eqs 1</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>, the discharge capacity is lower than expected for the reaction described by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>, but is larger assuming a pathway according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>. However, solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation should lead to a discharge capacity larger than the theoretical value, making a reaction according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a> highly unlikely (i.e., the formation of Na<sub>3</sub>P can be most probably excluded). However, if only NaP is generated, the theoretical capacity of 1142 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> matches the experimental value, taking SEI formation into account. Moreover, a contribution to the specific capacity by cointercalation of solvent molecules from the electrolyte into the active material cannot be excluded and might be a possible explanation for the high specific capacity of the first discharge step. Therefore, a reaction mechanism according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>, in which the difference between expected and observed specific capacity is contributed by a combination of SEI formation and cointercalation of solvent molecules seems possible as well. However, the irreversible capacity decrease for the first cycle of 504 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> corresponds to a loss of ∼4.0 Na/f.u. Usually, the capacity loss between initial discharging and charging due to SEI formation is not as large as that observed, indicating a strongly altered reaction mechanism for the first charge process. The profiles of the following 131 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b) significantly differ from the first one and no distinct potential plateaus can be identified during early cycling stages: the potential steeply decreases from 3.0 V to 0.5 V, followed by a continuous reduction until the cell is discharged. In the charging curve, the potential significantly increases from 0.1 V to 2.1 V, where the slope flattens slightly with further rising potential, until the cell is completely charged. Toward the 132nd cycle, two pseudoplateaus in the discharge profile develop at ∼1.51 and 1.02 V, as well as at ∼1.56 and 1.87 V in the charge profile, accompanied by an increasing specific capacity. Another change of the discharge and charge curves occurs from the 132nd cycle to the 165th cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c). While the first pseudoplateau at 1.51 V does not show any changes, the second pseudoplateau at ∼1.02 V nearly disappeared in the discharge profile reaching the 165th cycle. The pseudoplateau in the charge curves at ∼1.56 V slightly shifts to 1.59 V and the plateau itself is somewhat smaller, whereas the pseudoplateau at 1.87 V shifts to 2.02 V and is considerably more pronounced in the 165th cycle, compared to the 132nd cycle. These alterations cause a decreasing specific capacity between the 132nd cycle and the 165th cycle. Finally, a further change of the curves is observed between the 165th cycle and the 206th cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d). While the pseudoplateau in the discharge profiles at ∼1.51 V is not affected, at lower potentials, a very weak pseudoplateau at ∼0.44 V develops. In the charge curves, the small pseudoplateau at 1.59 V becomes more intense and shifts to ∼1.57 V, while the broad pseudoplateau at ∼2.02 V shifts back to 1.89 V and is less pronounced again. These changes of the electrochemical behavior between the 165th cycle and the 206th cycle are accompanied again by an increase in specific capacity.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Electrochemical cycling performance and Coulombic efficiency of CrPS<sub>4</sub> test cells against Na. A current rate of 1 Ag<sup>–1</sup> was applied within a potential window of 3.0 <b>–</b> 0.1 V. Discharge and charge profiles of (b) cycles 1–132 (inset shows the first cycle), (c) cycles 132–165, and (d) cycles 165–206 of the CrPS<sub>4</sub> electrode. In panel (b), every 10th cycle is displayed; in panels (c) and (d), every 5th cycle is displayed.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14980&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The alterations of the potential plateaus are better identified in the d<i>Q/</i>d<i>V</i> curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a). All events already discussed above appear as well-pronounced reduction and oxidation signals. More interestingly, the d<i>Q/</i>d<i>V</i> curves clearly show that the 206th and 220th cycles exhibit identical signals for oxidation and reduction process, indicating that no further changes of the mechanism occur. Regarding the 300th cycle, no alterations for the reduction process can be detected, but the signal for the second oxidation process has changed from 1.89 V to ∼2.07 V. Since this signal is the only one changing, it is most likely an early sign of electrode degradation during long-term cycling. Nevertheless, after the 300th cycle, a high reversible specific discharge capacity of 687 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is obtained. The observations made here point toward a steadily changing reaction mechanism during long-term cycling. To account for not well-resolved d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves of the first cycle, additional CV measurements (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b) were performed. The CV curves clearly show four reduction processes but only two oxidation processes in the first cycle. The first and most intense reduction peak at ∼1.58 V (peak 1) is assigned to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into the interlayer space of CrPS<sub>4</sub>, which is accompanied by the reduction of Cr<sup>3+</sup>. The reduction of Cr<sup>2+/3+</sup> and Cr<sup>3+</sup> was recently confirmed to occur at a potential of ∼0.4 V for Cr<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and NiCr<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> against sodium. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> Therefore, we assign the reduction process at ∼0.53 V (peak 3) to the reduction of Cr<sup>2+</sup> to the metallic state. The origin of the reduction peaks occurring at ∼0.83 V (peak 2) and ∼0.26 V (peak 4) remains uncertain. A stepwise reduction of P<sup>5+</sup> as well as SEI formation are possible explanations for the occurrence of these peaks. During the charging process, an intensive oxidation peak is observed at ∼2.06 V, while a smaller and not well-resolved second oxidation event is detected at a potential of ∼2.55 V. Taking the initial capacity drop (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, inset) into account, we suggest a two-step oxidation of phosphorus to its starting oxidation state of P<sup>5+</sup> in the first charge process, while the oxidation of Cr<sup>0</sup> is kinetically hindered. The expected oxidation peak for Cr<sup>0</sup>/Cr<sup>3+</sup> is ∼1.2 V <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> or slightly above at ∼1.5 V, which potentially is the reason for the very small oxidation and reduction peaks in the CV curve (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b) at this potential. In the second and subsequent cycles, the oxidation process at ∼2.55 V disappears, which might be an indication that phosphorus is only oxidized to P<sup>3+</sup> after the first cycle. The CV curves of cycles 2–5 have an almost uniform appearance, but differ strongly from the first cycle, which is typical for a conversion mechanism. The CV curves exhibit only one peak for the reduction (∼0.19 V) and oxidation (∼2.04 V), which, in the context of our assumptions, is related to the oxidation and reduction of P<sup>0</sup>/P<sup>3+</sup>. The corresponding Na uptake amounts to 3.6 Na/f.u. in the second cycle, which is in agreement with the oxidation of P<sup>0</sup> to P<sup>3+</sup>, as well as a minor portion of Cr<sup>0</sup> to Cr<sup>3+</sup>. With increasing cycle number, deactivated Cr<sup>0</sup> gradually starts to participate in the electrochemical reaction again, potentially leading to the development of a second pseudoplateau in the charge profiles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b), as indicated by the peak in the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curve of the 132nd cycle at 1.56 V (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). Consequently, the specific capacity steadily increases until the majority of Cr is involved in the electrochemical reaction again. The corresponding uptake in the 132nd cycle is 5.8 Na/f.u., which is nearly the expected value of 6 Na/f.u. for the proposed reaction. Furthermore, after the majority of Cr is activated again, the electrochemical reaction obviously is subject to mechanistic changes, causing a temporary decrease in capacity, which is evidenced by the changes occurring in the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves of the 132nd, 165th, and 206th cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c, inset).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> analysis of selected cycles during long-term cycling, (b) CV curves recorded with a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> within a potential range between 3.0 V and 0.1 V and (c) d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves of the 132nd, 165th, and 206th cycles. The red and green arrows indicate the redox processes responsible for capacity decrease (red) and increase (green). (d) Rate capability at several current rates ranging from 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> to 3.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> for CrPS<sub>4</sub> test cells against Na within a potential window of 3.0 V to 0.1 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14980&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">However, it is noteworthy that CV curves and d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> analysis alone are not capable of providing deep insights into mechanistic aspects of the electrochemical reaction, but rather give indications about occurring redox processes. The reaction mechanism of the first cycle is discussed in more detail in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec3_3" aria-label="section 3.3">section 3.3</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Rate capability measurements (current rates = 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 1.5, 2.0, 3.0, and again 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, applied for 10 cycles each) are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d. The lowest rate (0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>) delivers a specific discharge capacity in the 10th cycle of 555 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼4.4 Na/f.u.), while increasing the current rate leads to lower values after 10 cycles (see <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>). We note that the initial capacity increases for every rate within the 10 cycles applied, and by switching back to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a capacity retention of 116% is reached. However, a decrease of capacity is observed after the first 5 cycles at this current rate. This decrease in capacity potentially has the same origin as the temporary reduced capacity during long-term cycling. In contrast, this capacity decrease occurs earlier in the rate capability experiments than in the long-term measurements, which might be caused by the high current rates overcoming the assumed kinetic hindrance more quickly, because of a higher energy input into the system. Nevertheless, the results indicate a good cycling stability at higher current rates, as well as an extraordinary capacity retention after applying high current rates.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Observed Specific Discharge Capacities at Different Current Rates and Corresponding Capacity Retentions</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table noteworthy that the electrochemical data measured for CrPS<sub>4</sub> is comparable with high-performance binary sulfides (see <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table 2</a>), even though those binary compounds are often improved by adding highly conductive additives, such as reduced graphene oxide (rGO) or carbon nanotubes (CNT) or are parts of other advanced carbonaceous composites.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2. Comparison between Selected Indicators of Electrochemical Performance of CrPS<sub>4</sub> and Binary Sulfide Anode Materials</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="/"><col align="char" char="/"><col align="center"></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">anode</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="/">long-term stability: capacity [mAh g<sup>–1</sup>]/current rate [mA&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>]/cycle number [#]</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="/">rate capability: capacity [mAh g<sup>–1</sup>]/current rate [mA&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>]</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">reference</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">CrPS<sub>4</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="/">687/1000/300</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="/">288/3000</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">this&nbsp;work</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">NiS/C<a class="ref internalNav" href="#t2fn1" aria-label="a"><sup>a</sup></a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="/">208/1475/500</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="/">250/2950</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56');
