return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C-G<a class="ref internalNav" href="#t2fn3" aria-label="c"><sup>c</sup></a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="/">478/100/100</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="/">306/2000</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">CoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>NF@GF<a class="ref internalNav" href="#t2fn4" aria-label="d"><sup>d</sup></a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="/">491/500/100</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="/">479/2000</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a></td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap-foot" id="IDtable-wrap-foot-t2fn1"><div class="footnote" id="t2fn1"><sup><sup>a</sup></sup><p class="last">Composite derived from parent MOF structure.</p></div><div class="footnote" id="t2fn2"><sup><sup>b</sup></sup><p class="last">Material embedded in porous carbon hollow spheres.</p></div><div class="footnote" id="t2fn3"><sup><sup>c</sup></sup><p class="last">3D carbon-wrapped iron sulfide interlocked graphene composite.</p></div><div class="footnote" id="t2fn4"><sup><sup>d</sup></sup><p class="last">Nanoflakes grown on graphite foam.</p></div></div></div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i15" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i15">3.3.  Investigations of the Reaction Mechanism</h3><div class="NLM_p">Electrodes of pressed powder were used to obtain a larger amount of material necessary for the variety of characterization methods. The initial discharge capacities are lower than for electrodes cast on Cu foil and the specific discharge capacities are lower than expected according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>, in contrast to film electrodes. Therefore, to be sure that the completely discharged state is reached, film electrodes were also prepared for characterization. The initial average discharge capacity for the film electrodes of 1167 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼9.2 Na/f.u.) is larger than that for powder electrodes (785 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>; ∼6.2 Na/f.u.) (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a> in the Supporting Information), but the discharge profiles are very similar (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> in the Supporting Information). Beside early termination of the electrochemical reaction in powder electrodes, the first electrochemical plateau occurs at slightly lower potentials (∼1.4 V) and decreases continuously in contrast to film electrodes, which exhibit a very stable potential plateau at ∼1.65 V. These deviations between the two electrode types are potentially caused by Na<sup>+</sup> transport limitations in the powder electrodes, because of higher mass loading (∼30.4 mg cm<sup>–1</sup> vs ∼0.88 mg cm<sup>–1</sup>). The average capacity for the first charge process (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>) is 774 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼6.1 Na/f.u.) for film electrodes, whereas, for powder electrodes, no complete cycle could be obtained (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S3–S6</a> in the Supporting Information for details on powder cells). An exemplary discharge/charge profile and corresponding sodium uptake and release of the CrPS<sub>4</sub> electrode is depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Exemplary depiction of discharge and charge profiles of a typical CrPS<sub>4</sub> film electrode during sodium uptake and release. The investigated discharge and charge states are marked as red dots.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14980&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div id="sec3_3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i16" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i16">3.3.1.  X-ray Diffraction Experiments</h4><div class="NLM_p">XRD patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>; see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> in the Suporting Information for full range) reveal a slight reduction of crystallinity upon uptake of 1 Na/f.u. as well as the appearance of new reflections at 1.7° 2θ and 1.8° 2θ (shoulder), which gain intensity after insertion of 2 Na/f.u. and the reflection at 2.2° 2θ becomes more intense at this stage. In addition, new reflections developed between 5.9° 2θ and 6.7° 2θ (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>), as well as a slight shift of the reflection of the starting material at 4.40° 2θ to 4.36° 2θ, indicating the formation of a new phase. After the uptake of 3 Na/f.u. reflections of the pristine material disappeared and the most intense reflection is now located at 4.35° 2θ, which is less intense than for 2 Na/f.u. During further Na insertion, the intensity of the reflections continuously decreases until no crystalline phase can be observed after an uptake of 6 Na/f.u. and only reflections of Na<sub>2</sub>S can be observed in the discharged state (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). Characterization of discharged powder cells indicate that the crystallization of Na<sub>2</sub>S starts between 6 and 6.2 Na/f.u. (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). In addition to these observations, some peculiarities should be briefly discussed: (i) the most intense reflections at 4.40° 2θ shifted to lower scattering angles; (ii) the final position is reached after 3 Na/f.u.; and (iii) the highest intensity of the new reflections is observed after an uptake of 2 Na/f.u.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. XRD patterns of the CrPS<sub>4</sub> electrode collected at different discharge states compared to the pattern of Na<sub>2</sub>S calculated from literature data. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a></p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14980&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In the following analysis of the XRD data, we assume that the crystal symmetry is not changed during Na uptake. We are aware that this is not necessarily the case, as demonstrated especially for transition-metal dichacolgenides, where, in the equilibrium state, different structure types and even superstructures were observed. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62 ref63 ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62 ref63 ref64" aria-label="reference 62 to 64">(62−64)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">A closer look at the development of the XRD patterns at the early discharge states (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>) evidences the formation and development of new reflections at 12.9° 2θ and 13.7° 2θ, which strongly suggest an intercalation process. The first new reflection at 12.9° 2θ (<i>d</i> ≈ 6.90 Å) occurs after insertion of 1 Na/f.u., while the second one is observed as a shoulder and is only clearly visible after the uptake of 2 Na/f.u. at 13.7° 2θ (<i>d</i> ≈ 6.50 Å). For pristine CrPS<sub>4</sub>, the <i>d</i>-value of the (001) planes is calculated as ∼6.14 Å, using <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq 3</a>:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i17"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span>A possible candidate for a Na inserted intermediate phase is Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> with a <i>c</i>-axis of 7.075 Å, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> which is very similar to the <i>d</i>-value of 6.90 Å of the first observed new reflection. Despite the differing crystal structures between Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> and CrPS<sub>4</sub> the expansion of the <i>c</i>-axis of CrPS<sub>4</sub> is a clear hint for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation. Simultaneously with the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation, the (200) reflection gains intensity, because of the scattering power of Na in the van der Waals gaps. Along with this change, the intensity of reflections in stacking direction increases and shifts toward larger <i>d</i>-values. Assuming that the symmetry of the structure was not changed during Na insertion, we assign the reflection at <i>d</i> = 6.90 Å as (001) of the intercalated compound. For transition-metal dichalcogenides, it was proposed that the expansion of layers by intercalation of A<sup>+</sup> cations is proportional to the radius of A<sup>+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref65'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a> In addition to this geometrical effect, electronic effects cannot be neglected, such as, e.g., repulsive interactions between the negatively charged layers after uptake of the electrons during intercalation. The interlayer expansion of Δ<i>c</i> = 0.71 Å observed after insertion of 1 Na/f.u. is lower than expected, considering the effective ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> in 6-fold coordination geometry of 1.02 Å, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref66'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref66" aria-label="reference 66">(66)</a> pointing to an intercalation of ca. 0.7 Na/f.u. For the reflection located at <i>d</i> = 6.50 Å, Δ<i>c</i> amounts to 0.31 Å, i.e., these domains contain a lower Na content. However, these explanations are based on the assumption that no structural phase transitions occur as observed, e.g., for Na intercalation in TiS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref67'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref67" aria-label="reference 67">(67)</a></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. XRD patterns of the CrPS<sub>4</sub> electrode collected at early states of discharge. 2θ ranges of 12°–17.5° 2θ, 31°–35° 2θ. and 42°–53° 2θ are shown. Intensities of the pristine sample were divided by 4 for better visibility.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14980&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Indeed, some further changes are observed in different regions of the XRD patterns, which may indicate a phase transition and/or shifting of the slabs. But one should also keep in mind that electron transfer leads to a reduction of Cr<sup>3+</sup> (d<sup>3</sup>) to Cr<sup>2+</sup> (d<sup>4</sup>), changing the ionic radius and, consequently, the Cr–S bond length affecting reflection positions and intensities. The decreasing intensities after an uptake of 2 Na/f.u. are caused by significant reduction of coherently scattering domains and finally amorphization, which is completed after the uptake of 5–6 Na/f.u. (compare <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical intercalation results in host–guest complexes, which are not in equilibrium, considering the intercalation time. Increasing the waiting time after Na uptake leads to a relaxation similar to annealing at room temperature and changes of reflection intensities can be expected. This was investigated for a sample after insertion of 2 Na/f.u. (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>). Several alterations occur within 90 h of relaxation time: (i) the (001) reflection of CrPS<sub>4</sub> loses intensity; (ii) the two reflections indicative for intercalated CrPS<sub>4</sub> become more intense; (iii) the intensity of the (200) reflection slightly increases; and (iv) no shift of the reflection position can be observed. This behavior might be explained by initial intercalation into edge regions of CrPS<sub>4</sub> domains and subsequent slow movement of Na<sup>+</sup> from these regions into the differently intercalated phases, thus reducing the amount of pristine CrPS<sub>4</sub>.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Time-dependent XRD measurements of the CrPS<sub>4</sub> electrode after insertion of 2Na/f.u. depicted (a) on top of each other and (b) stacked for better visibility. The (001) and (200) reflections of the pristine CrPS<sub>4</sub> are labeled.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14980&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Summarizing the findings led to the following conclusions:<table class="listgroup" border="0" width="95%" list-type="label"><tbody><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(i)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">Na is intercalated into the van der Waals gaps of CrPS<sub>4</sub> during early stages of discharge, which was already suggested by the plateau in the discharge curve (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, inset).</p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(ii)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">The occurrence of two intense reflections at 2 Na/f.u. indicates the formation of domains containing different amounts of Na within the interlayer space visualized by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq 4</a>.</p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(iii)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">Beyond 3 Na/f.u., first domain sizes of CrPS<sub>4</sub> are successively reduced followed by amorphization and formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S. After a complete cycle, no distinct reflections can be observed in the XRD pattern.</p></td></tr></tbody></table><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i18"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">The loss of crystallinity, as well as the observed structural alterations during the electrochemical reaction, are confirmed by PDF data (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>; see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> in the Supporting Information for full range). After uptake of 6 Na/f.u., the spatial extension is decreased to ∼7 Å (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>) and no peaks corresponding to characteristic interatomic distances of CrPS<sub>4</sub> are detected. Only three distinct signals are left and two of them (1.42 and 2.44 Å) most likely correspond to amorphous carbon. During the uptake of Na up to 3 Na/f.u., rather subtle changes occur in the PDF curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>a). The most pronounced alterations are the decreasing intensities of the signals at 2.04, 3.73, 4.32, 7.22, 11.02, and 13.01 Å, as well as increasing intensities at 2.84, 3.42, 6.87, and 12.01 Å. The PDF curve for 3 Na/f.u. exhibits only a few sharp signals at small distances up to ∼4 Å, while, at larger distances, the signals are rather broad but still fit the distances of CrPS<sub>4</sub> quite well, indicating residual CrPS<sub>4</sub>. On the other hand, a signal of an intercalation compound is expected at ∼3.5 Å, according to XRD investigations. Indeed, increasing intensities at 3.42 Å for 2 Na/f.u. is a clear hint for the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into CrPS<sub>4</sub> resulting in one or more Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CrPS<sub>4</sub> phases (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq 4</a>). XRD experiments suggested that the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S sets in after the uptake of 6 Na/f.u., which is confirmed by signals developing after this stage. However, only some of these signals match the calculated distances of Na<sub>2</sub>S, which is caused by the highly defective nature of this compound (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>b). For the fully discharged sample, only few signals are clearly developed. The signals at 2.41 and 2.83 Å match calculated PDF curves of Na<sub>2</sub>S (2.83 Å: Na–S), Cr (2.50/2.88 Å: Cr–Cr), and NaF (2.32 Å: Na–F), as well as CrP (2.38/2.77 Å: Cr–P/Cr-Cr). The signal at 3.28 Å might correspond to the Na–Na distance of NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>S. The PDF curve of a full cycled electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>b) is very similar to that of the discharged sample and only significant changes occurred for the intensity ratio of the signals at 2.41 and 2.83 Å, i.e., the former is much more intense than the latter indicating formation of phases with similar distances during charging. In addition, the intensity of the peak at 2.83 Å decreases but does not disappear, which is a hint that phases present in the discharged state do not fully participate in the charge process. The formation of a shoulder at 2.04 Å and the increasing intensity at 2.41 Å indicate the recovery of CrPS<sub>4</sub> (2.01/2.09 Å: P–S; 2.40/2.48 Å: Cr–S) on a local scale. At this stage of analyses of the PDF curves, it becomes clear that the expected complex mixture prevents assignment of distinct phases. To summarize the complex reactions, the XRD and PDF data indicate the formation of different compounds on the nanoscale and only Na<sub>2</sub>S could be identified for &gt;6 Na/f.u. However, after an intercalation reaction at the beginning (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq 4</a>), a general conversion mechanism according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a> generating amorphous Cr and Na<sub>2</sub>S, as well as SEI formation accompanied by the formation of NaF is most probable, while recharging might lead to recovery of the local environment of CrPS<sub>4</sub>.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Pair distribution function (PDF) of CrPS<sub>4</sub> electrodes after uptake of several amounts of Na/f.u. (a) PDF in the range of 1.5–7 Å. The uptake of 1–6 Na/f.u. as well as the starting material, pure carbon and the PDF calculated from the structure of CrPS<sub>4</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> is shown. (b) PDF in the range of 1.5–7 Å, showing the starting material, the uptake of 6 Na/f.u. and the discharged film electrode (+9.2Na/f.u.), and the PDF after a complete cycle (−6.1 Na/f.u.). For comparison, PDFs calculated from the structures of Na<sub>2</sub>S, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a> CrP, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref68'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref68" aria-label="reference 68">(68)</a> bcc Cr, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref69'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref69" aria-label="reference 69">(69)</a> and NaF <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref70'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref70" aria-label="reference 70">(70)</a> are shown as well.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14980&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i19" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i19">3.3.2.  X-ray Absorption Experiments</h4><div class="NLM_p">The X-ray absorption near-edge spectra (XANES) were collected at the Cr K edge at different states of discharge and first derivatives were used to determine XANES features and edge energy positions (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>). Pristine CrPS<sub>4</sub> exhibits a weak pre-edge feature at 5989.1 eV, because of the dipole-forbidden but quadrupole-allowed 1s → 3d transition and the Cr K-edge is located at 5996.1 eV. A clearly defined white line (1s → 4p transition) can be observed at 6011.2 eV.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0009.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. (a) XANES spectra at the Cr K-edge of CrPS<sub>4</sub> electrodes at different states of discharge and (b) the corresponding first derivatives. (c) XANES spectra of the pristine CrPS<sub>4</sub>, compared with the electrode after a complete discharge and a full cycle, as well as bcc Cr foil, and (d) the corresponding first derivatives.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14980&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">During Na uptake (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 9</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>b), the Cr–K edge shifts to lower energy (5995.8 eV) and gains intensity after insertion of 2 Na/f.u., while the 1s → 4p transition exhibits a slight decrease in intensity (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>a). Further Na uptake leads to rotation around an isosbestic point at 5997.2 eV, accompanied by a steady decrease of the edge energy due to the reduction of Cr<sup>3+</sup>, until no clearly defined edge feature is detectable, while the first derivative (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>b) displays a signal at ∼5994.2 eV. Simultaneously, the pre-edge signal slightly shifts toward 5988.7 eV gaining intensity and reaching maximum intensity at the discharged state, resembling that of nanocrystalline elemental Cr. The differences between the spectrum of the discharged sample and the Cr foil is a phenomenon known for metal nanoparticles and amorphous alloys <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54 ref71 ref72 ref73'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54 ref71 ref72 ref73" aria-label="reference 54 and 71 to 73">(54,71−73)</a> caused by strong disorder and large amounts of surface atoms/defects altering scattering paths, compared to well-crystalline phases. At this point of data analysis, the formation of CrP instead of elemental Cr cannot be ruled out, because the present spectrum shows similarities with that of literature data for crystalline CrP. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref74'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref74" aria-label="reference 74">(74)</a> In addition, investigation of transition-metal monophosphides MP (M = Cr, Mn, Fe, Co) have shown that the oxidation state of these compounds is better formulated as M<sup>1+</sup>P<sup>1–</sup> instead of M<sup>3+</sup>P<sup>3–</sup>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref75'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref75" aria-label="reference 75">(75)</a> and the reduction of Cr<sup>3+</sup> to a lower oxidation state is consistent with a possible CrP formation besides the generation of nanosized Cr.</div><div class="NLM_p">The analysis of the XRD and PDF data suggest that some residual CrPS<sub>4</sub> and an intercalated compound Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CrPS<sub>4</sub> is present after the uptake of 2 Na/f.u. The intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> clearly results in the reduction of constituents of CrPS<sub>4</sub>. Because the edge energy and the specific features on the rising edge are affected by the interatomic bond length (covalency) and defects, the assignment of a certain oxidation state is barely possible and phosphorus might be reduced upon intercalation of Na as well. However, based on the redox couples Cr<sup>3+</sup>/Cr<sup>2+</sup> and H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>/H<sub>4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub> (<i>E</i><sup>0</sup> = −0.424 V vs <i>E</i><sup>0</sup> = −0.933 V), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref76'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref76" aria-label="reference 76">(76)</a> we suggest that Cr<sup>3+</sup> is reduced before P<sup>5+</sup>. The data gathered so far demonstrate the high complexity of the present system, preventing an unambiguous assignment to distinct phases. However, based on the results from XRD, PDF, and XANES, the formation of intercalation compounds at the early discharge states can be safely stated. The occurrence of an isosbestic point after the uptake of 3 Na/f.u. and onward clearly demonstrates that no intermediates are formed during further discharging.</div><div class="NLM_p">After a complete cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 9</a>c and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>d), the XANES is nearly featureless, which is often observed for nanoparticles. In the first derivative (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>d), the signal at 5988.7 eV for the discharged sample decreased dramatically and shifted slightly to 5989.1 eV after recharging, while also a second peak at ∼5996 eV emerged for the recharged sample. These signals match with that of CrPS<sub>4</sub> and are in accordance with the findings of PDF data, suggesting that, during the charging, a nanoscopic phase was generated with a local structural order and electronic situation similar to that observed in CrPS<sub>4</sub>.</div><div class="NLM_p">The information gathered from XANES spectra is consistent with the results of the XRD investigations, as the formation of intermediate phases within the uptake of 2 Na/f.u. and its subsequent decomposition upon further Na insertion is observed in XRD patterns and is supported by XANES data. In addition, in the discharge process, a reduction of Cr<sup>3+</sup> occurs. Furthermore, the recovery of a material resembling the structural characteristics of the starting material after a full cycle is in accordance with PDF analysis.</div><div class="NLM_p">The EXAFS R-space plot (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>) of CrPS<sub>4</sub> shows an intense peak at 2.01 Å corresponding to Cr–S bonds in the CrS<sub>6</sub> octahedron, while the less intense signal at 2.54 Å is due to the first Cr–P distance. The other signals at 3.53 and 3.99 Å with a shoulder at 4.25 Å are assigned to the second Cr–P distance and different Cr–S separations, respectively. During the uptake of Na (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>a), the signal at 2.01 Å decreases and for 2–3 Na/f.u. distances beyond 2.5 Å almost disappeared. Nevertheless, weak newly formed signals (for 2 and 3 Na/f.u.) at ∼2.78, ∼3.26, ∼3.64, and ∼4.02 Å support the formation of an intercalation compound. Taking a closer look at the discharged state (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>b), less signals than for bcc Cr are detected, because of the nanoscopic/amorphous and strongly disordered nature of the particles. After a full cycle, distances of 2.52, 3.59, and 3.96 Å appeared and the intensity of the signal at 2.01 Å increased. Because these signals match those of CrPS<sub>4</sub>, the recovery of a local structure similar to that of CrPS<sub>4</sub> is very likely, which is also backed by PDF and XANES data.</div><figure id="fig10" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 10</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0010.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 10. (a) FT pseudoradial distances for Cr absorber atoms of CrPS<sub>4</sub> electrodes at different discharge/charge states. (b) Comparison of pRDF of the pristine, completely discharged, full-cycled electrode and Cr foil.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14980&amp;id=fig10"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i20" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i20">3.3.3.  Magic-Angle-Spinning Nuclear Magnetic Resonance Experiments</h4><div class="NLM_p">In order to further address the underlying reaction mechanism, <sup>23</sup>Na and <sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR studies were conducted (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 11</a>). For the pristine sample, the <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR spectrum reveals, as expected, only a flat baseline. For some of the sodiated samples, the <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 11</a>a) reveal a small narrow peak at −12.1 ppm (marked with an asterisk (*)), which can be assigned to residues of the electrolyte salt sodium triflate. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> After uptake of 2 Na/f.u., a new broad signal at −12.4 ppm is observed, demonstrating the formation of an intermediate phase. It has been shown that chemical shifts of −4.4 ppm can be associated with Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> as well as amorphous and crystalline Na<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>S<sub>6</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref77'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref77" aria-label="reference 77">(77)</a> However, this signal most likely arises from intercalation compounds, as indicated also by the other methods described above. In accordance with XRD investigations, the highest intensity of this signal is reached after the uptake of 2 Na/f.u. and disappears again upon further sodiation. In addition, the sodiated samples (4–6 Na/f.u.) show the successive formation of a broad signal in the range between +200 ppm and −100 ppm. The large width of this signal is probably caused by the presence of an increasing amount of magnetic compounds in proximity to the Na-containing phases. Similar broad signals have been observed for sodiation of CuV<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> and Ni<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>S<sub>6</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> At later stages of sodiation (starting at +6 Na/f.u.), a narrow signal appears at 49.5 ppm, which is in agreement with NMR spectra of pure, well-crystallized Na<sub>2</sub>S <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref78'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref78" aria-label="reference 25 and 78">(25,78)</a> revealing its formation during the investigated reaction. After the full discharge, the spectrum is dominated by this narrow signal, which shows that a larger amount of Na<sub>2</sub>S has been formed experiencing less influence of other phases. An additional small signal at 6.9 ppm appearing after full discharge reveals the formation of some NaF <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref78 ref79'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref78 ref79" aria-label="reference 78 and 79">(78,79)</a> caused by SEI formation. The formation of NaCrS<sub>2</sub> can be excluded since the spectrum of this compound would exhibit a strong peak at 1654 ppm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a> in the Supporting Information) which was not observed for any of the sodiated samples.</div><figure id="fig11" data-index="11" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 11</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_0011.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0011.jpeg" id="gr11" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 11. (a) <sup>23</sup>Na and (b) <sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR spectra of CrPS<sub>4</sub> for different sodiation states. The peak marked with an asterisk (*) in the <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR spectrum is caused by residues of the electrolyte salt sodium triflate.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/large/am1c14980_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14980&amp;id=fig11"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The <sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR investigations (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 11</a>b) show no signal for the starting material. This is surprising because the paramagnetic behavior of CrPS<sub>4</sub> at room temperature <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref80'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref80" aria-label="reference 80">(80)</a> should lead at least to the appearance of broad NMR signals. However, nonexistent signals might be the consequence of strong magnetic coupling within the CrPS<sub>4</sub> layers. After an uptake of 2 Na/f.u., a broad peak in the range between +200 and −100 ppm with its center at ∼90.8 ppm and smaller signals at 13.4 and 35.3 ppm were detected. Considering the results from XRD, this change in the spectrum is related to the alteration of the local environment of phosphorus due to the formation of two sodium intercalation compounds. The smaller signals disappeared after the uptake of 3 Na/f.u. and the broad signal shows some left shift, which might indicate ordering of sodium within the intercalation compounds and eventually a phase transition. After an uptake of 4 Na/f.u., new signals occurred at 39.4 and 15.2 ppm and after the insertion of 5 Na/f.u. another signal at ∼89.4 ppm has formed. This is consistent with isolated [PS<sub>4</sub>]<sup>3–</sup> tetrahedra. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref81 ref82'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref81 ref82" aria-label="reference 81 and 82">(81,82)</a> In addition, the broad underlying signal between +200 ppm and −100 ppm shows a small right shift and upon the uptake of 6 Na/f.u., the smaller signals slightly gain intensity. This signal occurring after the uptake of 5–6 Na/f.u. and disappearing for the completely discharged sample is similar to the signal of red phosphorus, in terms of chemical shift and shape (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 11</a>b). The disappearance of this signal is most likely caused by the formation of magnetic nanoparticles disrupting the signal or a high degree of structural disorder in the sample. The origin of the smaller and sharper signals is uncertain. Another expected phase to form upon discharging is Na<sub>3</sub>P, which was not observed here. The chemical shift for this phase is expected at approximately −208 ppm, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref83 ref84'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref83 ref84" aria-label="reference 83 and 84">(83,84)</a> which is not present in the <sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR and neither are the corresponding chemical shifts (75 and 100 ppm) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref83'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref83" aria-label="reference 83">(83)</a> in the <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The <sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR experiments reveal the complex nature of the reaction mechanism. Considering the obtained results of MAS NMR with XRD and XANES data reinforces the presence of several stages of an intercalation compound during early discharge resulting in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CrPS<sub>4</sub> phases. In this intercalation compound, the coupling within the layers is not as strong as in the starting material causing the observed sharp signals after uptake of 2 Na/f.u. <sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR experiments further suggest several phase transitions for P-containing phases throughout the discharge process and indicate the formation of elemental phosphorus after the uptake of 5 Na/f.u. Accordingly, <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR measurements indicate the formation of at least one new compound within the uptake of 2 Na/f.u. and further confirm the presence of Na<sub>2</sub>S and NaF for the completely discharged sample, which is in accordance with XRD and PDF analysis.</div></div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i21">4.  Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">In this study, we presented the electrochemical behavior of CrPS<sub>4</sub> and gave insights in the ongoing reaction mechanism. The thiophosphate CrPS<sub>4</sub> was successfully synthesized by chemical vapor transport using excess sulfur as a transport agent. The synthesis delivered highly crystalline and phase-pure CrPS<sub>4</sub>, as evidenced by XRD. Galvanostatic measurements of CrPS<sub>4</sub> electrodes in SIBs proved to exhibit extraordinary electrochemical performance with reversible specific capacities up to 687 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 300 cycles. Rate capability measurements showed a good tolerance against higher currents, as well as an exceptional capacity retention after high currents were applied. However, a temporary decrease of the specific capacity was observed, which is likely caused by the changing of the reaction mechanism, which was proven by d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> analysis. Beside these electrochemical investigations, we also have gained insights in the ongoing reaction mechanism during the initial sodium uptake and release. Despite the application of advanced characterization methods, we must note that the system investigated here turned out to be extremely complex and the obtained data are not unambiguous. XRD and <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR clearly proved the presence of Na<sub>2</sub>S in the discharged state, which was supported by PDF analysis, while XANES indicated the reduction of Cr(III) and suggested that nanoscopic and probably strongly disordered Cr was formed during discharging. Furthermore, signs of NaF were found by <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR, which is most likely caused by SEI formation. XRD measurements of the early discharge states (0–3 Na/f.u.) show strong indications of the formation of several intercalation compounds, which is also supported by PDF, XANES, EXAFS, and MAS NMR investigations. The obtained specific discharge capacity corresponds to an uptake of 9.2 Na/f.u. As no Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P phases were detected, the reaction likely proceeds according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a> (consumption of 8 Na/f.u. in total), while intermediate formation occurs as described in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq 4</a>. The observed difference in sodium uptake (1.2 Na/f.u.) could be caused by SEI formation, together with cointercalation of solvent molecules into the layered structure. In summary, the reaction mechanism might be summarized accordingly as follows:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq5"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/asset/images/medium/am1c14980_m005.gif" alt="" id="_i22"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(5)</span></div></div></span>Nevertheless, <sup>31</sup>P MAS NMR clearly reveals the complexity of the occurring reaction mechanism. Therefore, we note that a simple chemical equation as provided in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="eq 5">eq 5</a> is not suited to describe this complex system properly and should only be seen as a simplified model and for visualization purposes.</div><div class="NLM_p last">PDF, XANES, and EXAFS investigations of the charge process suggest that a nanoscopic local environment and electronic structure similar to CrPS<sub>4</sub> is recovered after the first cycle. The obtained charge capacity corresponds to only 6.1 Na/f.u., which might be caused by, e.g., Na<sub>2</sub>S partly not participating in the charging process, because of an incomplete oxidation of Cr or P during charging. In this context, CV curves suggest that most of the metallic Cr might be passivated during early cycling. Hence, only a minor portion of Cr is oxidized in the first cycle and merely vague signs of recovered CrPS<sub>4</sub> are detectable.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i23">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge <i>in the online version of the paper</i>. Results of EDX measurements and Raman spectroscopy of pristine CrPS<sub>4</sub>; comparison of film vs powder electrodes; full view of XRD and PDF data of CrPS<sub>4</sub> electrodes at different discharge/charge state; <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR spectrum of NaCrS<sub>2</sub>. The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c14980?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c14980</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">(<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf">am1c14980_si_001.pdf (1.14 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c14980" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Mobile and stationary energy storage by rechargeable batteries is a topic of broad societal and economical relevance.  Lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. is at the forefront of the development, but a massively growing market will likely put severe pressure on resources and supply chains.  Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been reconsidered with the aim of providing a lower-cost alternative that is less susceptible to resource and supply risks.  On paper, the replacement of lithium by sodium in a battery seems straightforward at first, but unpredictable surprises are often found in practice.  What happens when replacing lithium by sodium in electrode reactions.  This review provides a state-of-the art overview on the redox behavior of materials when used as electrodes in lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries, resp.  Advantages and challenges related to the use of sodium instead of lithium are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrGeYJemvxIpbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj7Wqqo1Tiiow"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhvVKmurvE&amp;md5=4342a80be51730e08dc9faec2f79fb63</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701428"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.-B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.-K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.-X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-Ion Batteries: From Academic Research to Practical Commercialization</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards greener and more sustainable batteries for electrical energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Larcher, D.; Tarascon, J.-M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Chemistry</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.  With the same themes in mind, the authors also highlight current and future electrochem. storage systems beyond Li-ion batteries.  The complexity and importance of recycling battery materials is also discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGph4-d2B3Q8DLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj7Wqqo1Tiiow"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFKlsrjF&amp;md5=977f0fc171bb41af30457715fc0ded72</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1016/j.rser.2010.11.037"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Panwar, N.                 10.1002/smll.201502788</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D12%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D1359-1368%26journal%3DSmall%26issue%3D10%26author%3DS.%2BPeng%26author%3DX.%2BHan%26author%3DL.%2BLi%26author%3DZ.%2BZhu%26author%3DF.%2BCheng%26author%3DM.%2BSrinivansan%26author%3DS.%2BAdams%26author%3DS.%2BRamakrishna%26title%3DUnique%2BCobalt%2BSulfide%252FReduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%2BComposite%2Bas%2Ban%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%2Bwith%2BSuperior%2BRate%2BCapability%2Band%2BLong%2BCycling%2BStability%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fsmll.201502788&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c14980&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fsmll.201502788&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Unique Cobalt Sulfide/Reduced Graphene Oxide Composite as an Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries with Superior Rate Capability and Long Cycling Stability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Peng, Shengjie; Han, Xiaopeng; Li, Linlin; Zhu, Zhiqiang; Cheng, Fangyi; Srinivansan, Madhavi; Adams, Stefan; Ramakrishna, Seeram</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1359-1368</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SMALBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1613-6810</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Exploitation of high-performance anode materials is essential but challenging to the development of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Among all proposed anode materials for SIBs, sulfides were proved promising candidates due to their unique chem. and phys. properties.  A facile solvothermal method to in situ decorate cobalt sulfide (CoS) nanoplates on reduced graphene oxide (rGO) to build CoS@rGO composite is described.  When evaluated as anode for SIBs, an impressive high specific capacity (540 mAh/g at 1 A/g), excellent rate capability (636 mAh/g at 0.1 A/g and 306 mAh/g at 10 A/g), and extraordinarily cycle stability (420 mAh/g at 1 A/g after 1000 cycles) were demonstrated by CoS@rGO composite for sodium storage.  The synergetic effect between the CoS nanoplates and rGO matrix contributes to the enhanced electrochem. performance of the hybrid composite.  The results provide a facile approach to fabricate promising anode materials for high-performance SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGotD1r43G7-ibVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljreL3XL5Mzhw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xns1Cjtw%253D%253D&amp;md5=7834a86a1d3c3333ee7594239160e829</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sandwich-like Cobalt Sulfide-Graphene Composite - an Anode Material with Excellent Electrochemical Performance for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">88</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">71644</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">71651</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C5RA12478F</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D5%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D71644-71651%26journal%3DRSC%2BAdv.%26issue%3D88%26author%3DQ.%2BZhou%26author%3DL.%2BLiu%26author%3DG.%2BGuo%26author%3DZ.%2BYan%26author%3DJ.%2BTan%26author%3DZ.%2BHuang%26author%3DX.%2BChen%26author%3DX.%2BWang%26title%3DSandwich-like%2BCobalt%2BSulfide-Graphene%2BComposite%2B-%2Ban%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bwith%2BExcellent%2BElectrochemical%2BPerformance%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC5RA12478F&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c14980&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC5RA12478F&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sandwich-like cobalt sulfide-graphene composite - an anode material with excellent electrochemical performance for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhou, Qian; Liu, Li; Guo, Guoxiong; Yan, Zichao; Tan, Jinli; Huang, Zhifeng; Chen, Xiaoying; Wang, Xianyou</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">88</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">71644-71651</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">RSCACL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2046-2069</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, sodium-ion batteries have been considered alternatives to lithium-ion batteries.  However, the poor cycling performance and unsatisfactory rate capability of existing anodes hinder the development of sodium-ion batteries.  Here we fabricate a sandwich-like cobalt sulfide-reduced graphene oxide (CoS/rGO) composite using a hydrothermal method as the anode material for sodium ion batteries.  According to the SEM anal., CoS nanoparticles anchor on both sides of the reduced graphene oxide nanosheets in such a way that the nanoporous structure with a large amt. of void spaces can be prepd.  And the cycling performance of the sandwich-like CoS/rGO composite is drastically enhanced compared with that of the bare CoS nanoparticles.  After 100 cycles, the discharge capacity of CoS/rGO still remains at 230 mA h g-1, while the specific capacity of the bare CoS nanoparticles at the first cycle is 601 mA h g-1, dropping rapidly to 68 mA h g-1 after only 40 cycles.  Furthermore, CoS/rGO gives an excellent rate capability even up to a large current of 2 A g-1.  It is noted that the synergistic effect between CoS and graphene can contribute to the improved electrochem. performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq-eSWKiUzXs7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljreL3XL5Mzhw"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of One-Dimensional Copper Sulfide Nanorods as High-Performance Anode in Lithium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xue; He, Xinyi; Shi, Chunmei; Liu, Bo; Zhang, Yiyong; Wu, Shunqing; Zhu, Zizong; Zhao, Jinbao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemSusChem</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nanorod-like CuS and Cu2S have been fabricated by a hydrothermal approach without using any surfactant and template.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Conversion Reaction of Copper Sulfide Based Nanohybrids for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Na Rae; Choi, Jaewon; Yoon, Hyeon Ji; Lee, Min Eui; Son, Seung Uk; Jin, Hyoung-Joon; Yun, Young Soo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Sustainable Chemistry &amp; Engineering</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Intercalation-based anode materials for Na-ion batteries show relatively unfavorable electrochem. performances compared with those of Li-ion batteries because of the larger and heavier Na ion, as well as its higher electrode potential.  In contrast, conversion-reaction-based anode materials have great potential for use in Na-ion batteries.  In this study, copper sulfide nanodisks (CuS-NDs) were fabricated by a simple low-temp. reaction and applied as the anode materials for Na-ion batteries with acid-treated single-walled carbon nanotubes (a-SWCNTs), which act as a paperlike nanohybrid.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered nickel sulfide (NS)-reduced graphene oxide (RGO) composites are prepd. via a simple microwave-assisted method and subsequent annealing in N2/H2 atmosphere.  A detailed array of characterization tools are used to study their morphol., structure and electrochem. performance.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nickel Sulfide/Nitrogen-Doped Graphene Composites: Phase-Controlled Synthesis and High Performance Anode Materials for Lithium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mahmood, Nasir; Zhang, Chenzhen; Hou, Yanglong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A phase-controlled Ni3S4/nitrogen-doped graphene composite was synthesized by a hydrothermal method.  The further annealing process at 350° could lead to the formation of NiS1.03/nitrogen-doped graphene, another high-temp. stable phase of nickel sulfide.  Annealing treatment increased the electrochem. coupling between active nickel sulfide nanoparticles and nitrogen-doped graphene.  The percentage of pyridinic nitrogen increased up to 81.93% after annealing, which is favorable for the high electrochem. performance of nitrogen-doped graphene in lithium-ion batteries.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">TiS2 as an Advanced Conversion Electrode for Sodium-Ion Batteries with Ultra-High Capacity and Long-Cycle Life</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tao Hongwei; Zhou Min; Wang Ruxing; Wang Kangli; Cheng Shijie; Jiang Kai; Tao Hongwei; Wang Ruxing; Jiang Kai</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Titanium disulfide (TiS2) is investigated as an advanced conversion electrode for sodium (Na)-ion batteries (NIB) in an ether-based electrolyte (NaPF6/glyme (DME)).  The as-prepared TiS2 demonstrates a high reversible capacity of 1040 mA h g(-1) at 0.2 A g(-1) with the capacity contribution of 521 mA h g(-1) in the voltage region below 1.0 V (vs Na/Na(+)), remarkable initial coulombic efficiency of 95.9% and superior rate capability of 621 mA h g(-1) at 40 A g(-1).  The high conductivity of the Ti-based compounds and nanosized particles generated by chemical conversion reactions could minimize the entropic barrier for the reversible conversion, resulting in high reversibility and ultrafast charge/discharge ability of the electrode.  Moreover, with its strong ability to adsorb soluble polysulfide intermediates, the as-prepared TiS2 electrode exhibits superior cycling stability over 9000 cycles, serving as a stable and ultra-high capacity conversion electrode for NIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcQjrtMkf2pHZ6__Jf7-eMvJfW6udTcc2eZN5IM80vk4jbntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BB3crltFCktg%253D%253D&amp;md5=48e36cf3ea11dca2481b0691679cd71c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201601329"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, S.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Elucidation of the Sodium - Copper Extrusion Mechanism in CuCrS2: A High Capacity, Long-Life Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Krengel, Markus; Hansen, Anna-Lena; Hartmann, Felix; van Dinter, Jonas; Bensch, Wolfgang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Batteries &amp; Supercaps</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">176-183</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The compd. CuCrS2 with a quasi-layered crystal structure was investigated as room temp. rechargeable sodium-ion battery electrode.  It exhibits excellent performance as anode material with a high reversible capacity of 424 mAh g-1 at 700 mA g-1 after 200 cycles and a capacity retention of 98.6% compared to the third cycle.  Results of ex-situ X-Ray diffraction expts. performed at different stages of the discharge process demonstrate that at the beginning of Na uptake, Cu+ cations are reduced to nanosized metallic Cu particles which are expelled from the host lattice.  Simultaneously, Na is inserted into the host material leading to the formation of Na0.7Cu0.15CrS2 with significantly expanded interlayer space.  Metallic Cu and Na0.7Cu0.15CrS2 coexist at this stage of discharge.  Increasing the amt. of Na per formula unit leads to successive conversion to X-ray amorphous Cr, nanocryst. Na2S and metallic Cu.  The formation of highly disordered metallic Cr with domain sizes in the range of few nanometers is revealed by at. pair distribution function anal.  During the charge process, the nanocryst. Cu particles are retained and Na0.7Cu0.15CrS2 is at least partially reformed.  The finely distributed Cu particles dramatically improve the long-time stability as evidenced by comparison of the electrochem. behavior of mere NaCuCrS2.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGphlHa32GMbjLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liuUqmyqBD9Dg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXjsV2rtb4%253D&amp;md5=7301bcd375de136387b1ba1337df2734</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1039/C5CC04022A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Krengel, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Adelhelm, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Klein, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bensch, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">FeV<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> as a High Capacity Electrode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent progress on sodium ion batteries: potential high-performance anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Li; Zheng, Yang; Zhang, Shilin; Yang, Jianping; Shao, Zongping; Guo, Zaiping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Due to massively growing demand arising from energy storage systems, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been recognized as the most attractive alternative to the current commercialized lithium ion batteries (LIBs) owing to the wide availability and accessibility of sodium.  Unfortunately, the low energy d., inferior power d. and poor cycle life are still the main issues for SIBs in the current drive to push the entire technol. forward to meet the benchmark requirements for commercialization.  Over the past few years, tremendous efforts have been devoted to improving the performance of SIBs, in terms of higher energy d. and longer cycling lifespans, by optimizing the electrode structure or the electrolyte compn.  In particular, among the established anode systems, those materials, such as metals/alloys, phosphorus/phosphides, and metal oxides/sulfides/selenides, that typically deliver high theor. sodium-storage capacities have received growing interest and achieved significant progress.  Although some review articles on electrodes for SIBs have been published already, many new reports on these anode materials are constantly emerging, with more promising electrochem. performance achieved via novel structural design, surface modification, electrochem. performance testing techniques, etc.  So, we herein summarize the most recent developments on these high-performance anode materials for SIBs in this review.  Furthermore, the different reaction mechanisms, the challenges assocd. with these materials, and effective approaches to enhance performance are discussed.  The prospects for future high-energy anodes in SIBs are also discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrQBTNlAIykC7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj4wrgjG8gsKQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhtlOjt7jK&amp;md5=e4580fd3c5fcf8eb8cfa08a8dc758f49</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1039/C7TA10500B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.     <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3284-3303</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted great attention recently due to the abundance of sodium resources, particularly for large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grids.  More and more nanostructured anode materials have been developed with the aims of high energy d., high cycling stability, and excellent rate capability, in which two-dimensional (2D) nanostructures are showing promise due to their shortened paths for sodium ion transportation and larger surface areas for sodium ion absorption.  Moreover, 2D materials (e.g. graphene) have been proved to be excellent supporting and conducting agents in SIB anodes due to their high elec. cond. and structural stability, in which synergetic effects between the graphene and the active materials are generally obsd.  This review is devoted to the recent progress in the use of 2D active materials and in composites consisting of both 2D supports and active materials as anodes for SIBs.  Based on the manner of sodium storage, their electrochem. performance for sodium storage is discussed in terms of four classifications, including carbonaceous materials (graphene and carbon nanosheets), alloy based materials (Sn, Sb, and P), conversion materials (phosphides/oxides/sulfides/selenides), and intercalation materials (Ti-based compds.).  Finally, the main challenges for and perspectives on 2D nanostructures for sodium storage are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpWllz-lZVaobVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj4wrgjG8gsKQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhslartL4%253D&amp;md5=7fdf78c15bb6c1a542c2b6cf0e6780b5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Pulverization-Tolerance and Capacity Recovery of Copper Sulfide for High-Performance Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Park Jae Yeol; Kim Sung Joo; Dae Kyun Seong; Dao Khoi Phuong; Park Ji Su; Jeong Han Beom; Chang Joon Ha; Seo Hyeon Kook; Yuk Jong Min; Yim Kanghoon; Lee Yonghee; Ahn Chi Won</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>
