        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, sodium-ion batteries have been considered alternatives to lithium-ion batteries.  However, the poor cycling performance and unsatisfactory rate capability of existing anodes hinder the development of sodium-ion batteries.  Here we fabricate a sandwich-like cobalt sulfide-reduced graphene oxide (CoS/rGO) composite using a hydrothermal method as the anode material for sodium ion batteries.  According to the SEM anal., CoS nanoparticles anchor on both sides of the reduced graphene oxide nanosheets in such a way that the nanoporous structure with a large amt. of void spaces can be prepd.  And the cycling performance of the sandwich-like CoS/rGO composite is drastically enhanced compared with that of the bare CoS nanoparticles.  After 100 cycles, the discharge capacity of CoS/rGO still remains at 230 mA h g-1, while the specific capacity of the bare CoS nanoparticles at the first cycle is 601 mA h g-1, dropping rapidly to 68 mA h g-1 after only 40 cycles.  Furthermore, CoS/rGO gives an excellent rate capability even up to a large current of 2 A g-1.  It is noted that the synergistic effect between CoS and graphene can contribute to the improved electrochem. performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq-eSWKiUzXs7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhBRyNpK1_8Gw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtlCktrvM&amp;md5=bc12554a9476281fdc7dd3fc7c7973eb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.201402862"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of One-Dimensional Copper Sulfide Nanorods as High-Performance Anode in Lithium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ChemSusChem</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">12</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">3328</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3333</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

The electrochem. behavior of CuS and Cu2S nanorod anodes for Li-ion batteries reveal that they exhibit stable Li-ion insertion/extn. reversibility and outstanding rate capability. In contrast, conversion-reaction-based anode materials have great potential for use in Na-ion batteries.  In this study, copper sulfide nanodisks (CuS-NDs) were fabricated by a simple low-temp. reaction and applied as the anode materials for Na-ion batteries with acid-treated single-walled carbon nanotubes (a-SWCNTs), which act as a paperlike nanohybrid.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The compd. CuCrS2 with a quasi-layered crystal structure was investigated as room temp. rechargeable sodium-ion battery electrode.  It exhibits excellent performance as anode material with a high reversible capacity of 424 mAh g-1 at 700 mA g-1 after 200 cycles and a capacity retention of 98.6% compared to the third cycle.  Results of ex-situ X-Ray diffraction expts. performed at different stages of the discharge process demonstrate that at the beginning of Na uptake, Cu+ cations are reduced to nanosized metallic Cu particles which are expelled from the host lattice.  Simultaneously, Na is inserted into the host material leading to the formation of Na0.7Cu0.15CrS2 with significantly expanded interlayer space.  Metallic Cu and Na0.7Cu0.15CrS2 coexist at this stage of discharge.  Increasing the amt. of Na per formula unit leads to successive conversion to X-ray amorphous Cr, nanocryst. Na2S and metallic Cu.  The formation of highly disordered metallic Cr with domain sizes in the range of few nanometers is revealed by at. pair distribution function anal.  During the charge process, the nanocryst. Cu particles are retained and Na0.7Cu0.15CrS2 is at least partially reformed.  The finely distributed Cu particles dramatically improve the long-time stability as evidenced by comparison of the electrochem. behavior of mere NaCuCrS2.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGphlHa32GMbjLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhTr0g6-GZ0ZQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXjsV2rtb4%253D&amp;md5=7301bcd375de136387b1ba1337df2734</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref16" class="rightTabRefNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit16" data-doi="10.1039/C5CC04022A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Krengel, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Adelhelm, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Klein, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bensch, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">FeV<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> as a High Capacity Electrode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Due to massively growing demand arising from energy storage systems, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been recognized as the most attractive alternative to the current commercialized lithium ion batteries (LIBs) owing to the wide availability and accessibility of sodium.  Unfortunately, the low energy d., inferior power d. and poor cycle life are still the main issues for SIBs in the current drive to push the entire technol. forward to meet the benchmark requirements for commercialization.  Over the past few years, tremendous efforts have been devoted to improving the performance of SIBs, in terms of higher energy d. and longer cycling lifespans, by optimizing the electrode structure or the electrolyte compn.  In particular, among the established anode systems, those materials, such as metals/alloys, phosphorus/phosphides, and metal oxides/sulfides/selenides, that typically deliver high theor. sodium-storage capacities have received growing interest and achieved significant progress.  Although some review articles on electrodes for SIBs have been published already, many new reports on these anode materials are constantly emerging, with more promising electrochem. performance achieved via novel structural design, surface modification, electrochem. performance testing techniques, etc.  So, we herein summarize the most recent developments on these high-performance anode materials for SIBs in this review.  Furthermore, the different reaction mechanisms, the challenges assocd. with these materials, and effective approaches to enhance performance are discussed.  The prospects for future high-energy anodes in SIBs are also discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrQBTNlAIykC7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhTr0g6-GZ0ZQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhtlOjt7jK&amp;md5=e4580fd3c5fcf8eb8cfa08a8dc758f49</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref18" class="rightTabRefNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit18" data-doi="10.1039/C7TA10500B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted great attention recently due to the abundance of sodium resources, particularly for large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grids.  More and more nanostructured anode materials have been developed with the aims of high energy d., high cycling stability, and excellent rate capability, in which two-dimensional (2D) nanostructures are showing promise due to their shortened paths for sodium ion transportation and larger surface areas for sodium ion absorption.  Moreover, 2D materials (e.g. graphene) have been proved to be excellent supporting and conducting agents in SIB anodes due to their high elec. cond. and structural stability, in which synergetic effects between the graphene and the active materials are generally obsd.  This review is devoted to the recent progress in the use of 2D active materials and in composites consisting of both 2D supports and active materials as anodes for SIBs.  Based on the manner of sodium storage, their electrochem. performance for sodium storage is discussed in terms of four classifications, including carbonaceous materials (graphene and carbon nanosheets), alloy based materials (Sn, Sb, and P), conversion materials (phosphides/oxides/sulfides/selenides), and intercalation materials (Ti-based compds.).  Finally, the main challenges for and perspectives on 2D nanostructures for sodium storage are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpWllz-lZVaobVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhTr0g6-GZ0ZQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhslartL4%253D&amp;md5=7fdf78c15bb6c1a542c2b6cf0e6780b5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref19" class="rightTabRefNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit19" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Abstr.: Sodium-ion batteries have been emerging as attractive technologies for large-scale elec. energy storage and conversion, owing to the natural abundance and low cost of sodium resources.  However, the development of sodium-ion batteries faces tremendous challenges, which is mainly due to the difficulty to identify appropriate cathode materials and anode materials.  In this review, the research progresses on cathode and anode materials for sodium-ion batteries are comprehensively reviewed.  We focus on the structural considerations for cathode materials and sodium storage mechanisms for anode materials.  With the worldwide effort, high-performance sodium-ion batteries will be fully developed for practical applications.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Finding suitable electrode materials is one of the challenges for the commercialization of a sodium ion battery due to its pulverization accompanied by high volume expansion upon sodiation.  Here, copper sulfide is suggested as a superior electrode material with high capacity, high rate, and long-term cyclability owing to its unique conversion reaction mechanism that is pulverization-tolerant and thus induces the capacity recovery.  Such a desirable consequence comes from the combined effect among formation of stable grain boundaries, semi-coherent boundaries, and solid-electrolyte interphase layers.  The characteristics enable high cyclic stability of a copper sulfide electrode without any need of size and morphological optimization.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The ternary compd. CuV2S4 exhibits an excellent performance as anode material for sodium ion batteries with a high reversible capacity of 580 mAh g-1 at 0.7 A g-1 after 300 cycles.  A Coulombic efficiency of ≈99% is achieved after the third cycle.  Increase of the C-rate leads to a drop of the capacity, but a full recovery is obsd. after switching back to the initial C-rate.  In the early stages of Na uptake first Cu+ is reduced and expelled from the electrode as nanocryst. metallic Cu.  An increase of the Na content leads to a full conversion of the material with nanocryst. Cu particles and elemental V embedded in a Na2S matrix.  The formation of Na2S is evidenced by 23Na MAS NMR spectra and X-ray powder diffraction.  During the charge process the nanocryst. Cu particles are retained, but no cryst. materials are formed.  At later stages of cycling the reaction mechanism changes which is accompanied by the formation of copper(I) sulfide.  The presence of nanocryst. metallic Cu and/or Cu2S improves the elec. cond., leading to superior cycling and rate capability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoMziqiKsw51LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgHM5XkykLNoA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXpslCks78%253D&amp;md5=f7528c5f003ac80831828dc4c6c97021</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref26" class="rightTabRefNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit26" data-doi="10.1039/c3cp52125g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Klein, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jache, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bhide, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Adelhelm, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Conversion Reactions for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Phys.                         rightTab-cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Conversion reactions for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Klein, Franziska; Jache, Birte; Bhide, Amrtha; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Research on Na-ion batteries is focused on finding suitable electrode materials that enable cell reactions of high energy densities combined with low cost.  Naturally, an assessment of potential electrode materials requires a rational comparison with the analog reaction in Li-ion batteries.  The authors discuss the broad range of different conversion reactions for Na-ion batteries based on their basic thermodn. properties and compare them with their Li analogs.  Capacities, voltages, energy densities and vol. expansions are summarized to sketch out the scope for future studies in this research field.  For a given conversion electrode material, replacing Li by Na leads to a const. shift in cell potential ΔEo(Li-Na) depending on the material class.  For chlorides ΔEo(Li-Na) is nearly zero.  The theor. energy densities of conversion reactions of Na with fluorides or chlorides as pos. electrode materials typically reach values between 700 W h kg-1 and 1000 W h kg-1.  Next to the thermodn. assessment, results on several conversion reactions between Cu compds. (CuS, CuO, CuCl, CuCl2) and Na are being discussed.  Reactions with CuS and CuO were chosen because these compds. are frequently studied for conversion reactions with Li.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Searching for two-dimensional (2D) materials with multifunctionality is one of the main goals of current research in 2D materials.  Magnetism and semiconducting are certainly two desirable functional properties for a single 2D material.  In line with this goal, here we report a d. functional theory (DFT) study of bulk and single-layer magnetic semiconductor CrPS4.  We find that the ground-state magnetic structure of bulk CrPS4 exhibits the A-type antiferromagnetic ordering, which transforms to ferromagnetic (FM) ordering in single-layer CrPS4.  The calcd. formation energy and phonon spectrum confirm the stability of single-layer CrPS4.  The band gaps of FM single-layer CrPS4 calcd. with a hybrid d. functional are within the visible-light range.  We also study the effects of FM ordering on the optical absorption spectra and band alignments for water splitting, indicating that single-layer CrPS4 could be a potential photocatalyst.         </div><div class="casAbstract">2-Dimensional (2D) CrPS4 single crystals have been grown by the chem. vapor transport method.  The crystallog., magnetic, electronic, and thermal transport properties of the single crystals were investigated by the room-temp. X-ray diffraction, elec. resistivity ρ(T), sp. heat CP(T), and the electronic spin response (ESR) measurements.  CrPS4 crystals crystallize into a monoclinic structure.  The elec. resistivity ρ(T) shows a semiconducting behavior with an energy gap Ea = 0.166 eV.  The antiferromagnetic transition temp. is about TN = 36 K.  The spin flipping induced by the applied magnetic field is obsd. along the c axis.  The magnetic phase diagram of CrPS4 single crystal has been discussed.  The extd. magnetic entropy at TN is about 10.8 J/mol K, which is consistent with the theor. value R ln(2S + 1) for S = 3/2 of the Cr3+ ion.  Based on the mean-field theory, the magnetic exchange consts. J1 and Jc corresponding to the interactions of the intralayer and between layers are about 0.143 meV and -0.955 meV are obtained based on the fitting of the susceptibility above TN, which agree with the results obtained from the ESR measurements.  With the help of the strain for tuning the magnetic properties, monolayer CrPS4 may be a promising candidate to explore 2D magnetic semiconductors.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Atomically thin binary two-dimensional (2D) semiconductors exhibit diverse phys. properties depending on their compn., structure, and thickness.  By adding another element in these materials, which will give ternary 2D materials, the property and structure would greatly change and significantly expanded applications could be explored.  The authors report structural and optical properties of atomically thin Cr thiophosphate (CrPS4), a ternary antiferromagnetic semiconductor.  Its structural details were revealed by x-ray and electron diffraction.  TEM showed that preferentially cleaved edges are parallel to diagonal Cr atom rows, which readily identified their crystallog. orientations.  Strong in-plane optical anisotropy induced birefringence that also enabled efficient detn. of crystallog. orientation using polarized microscopy.  The lattice vibrations were probed by Raman spectroscopy and exhibited significant dependence on thickness of crystals exfoliated down to a single layer.  Optical absorption detd. by reflectance contrast was dominated by d-d-type transitions localized at Cr3+ ions, which was also responsible for the major luminescence peak at 1.31 eV.  The spectral features in the absorption and emission spectra exhibited noticeable thickness dependence and hinted at a high photochem. activity for single-layer CrPS4.  The current structural and optical study will provide a firm basis for future study and application of this kind of atomically thin magnetic semiconductors.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpDmK93HLgwZLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgiRt46uE-EhA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhslahurjO&amp;md5=42bf9af270a1225851fc0fdece2fb12d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref47" class="rightTabRefNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit47" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b01417"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jo, M.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ryu, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Crossover between Photochemical and Photothermal Oxidations of Atomically Thin Magnetic Semiconductor CrPS<sub>4</sub></span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>19</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">6</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">4043</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">4051</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Many two-dimensional (2D) semiconductors represented by transition metal dichalcogenides have tunable optical bandgaps in the visible or near IR-range standing as a promising candidate for optoelectronic devices.  Despite this potential, however, their photoreactions are not well understood or controversial in the mechanistic details.  In this work, we report a unique thickness-dependent photoreaction sensitivity and a switchover between two competing reaction mechanisms in atomically thin chromium thiophosphate (CrPS4), a two-dimensional antiferromagnetic semiconductor.  CrPS4 showed a threshold power d. 2 orders of magnitude smaller than that for MoS2 obeying a photothermal reaction route.  In addn., reaction cross section quantified with Raman spectroscopy revealed distinctive power dependences in the low and high power regimes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The pseudo-layered sulfide NiCr2S4 exhibits outstanding electrochem. performance as anode material in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  The Na storage mechanism is investigated by synchrotron-based X-ray scattering and absorption techniques as well as by electrochem. measurements.  A very high reversible capacity in the 500th cycle of 489 mAh g-1 is obsd. at 2.0 A g-1 in the potential window 3.0-0.1 V.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We demonstrate a general solid-state synthesis of nickel sulfide (NiS) and carbon-based composites (NiS/C) via simple thermal decompn. of nickel dibutyldithiocarbamate (C18H36N2NiS4) under ambient atm., which can be applied to various carbon-based materials such as 2D graphene nanosheets (GNSs), 1D carbon nanotubes (CNTs), and 0D carbon black (CB).  When used as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), the as-prepd. NiS/C composites demonstrate excellent sodium storage properties including superior cycle stability and rate capability, delivering reversible capacities of 483 (for NiS/GNSs), 394 (for NiS/CNTs), and 413 mAh/g (for NiS/CB) at a c.d. of 200 mA/g after 100 cycles, resp., which are much higher than that of the bare NiS counterpart (136 mAh/g at 200 mA/g after 100 cycles).  Moreover, reversible capacities of 372 mAh/g for NiS/GNSs, 331 mAh/g for NiS/CNTs, and 317 mAh/g for NiS/CB are realized at a high rate of 2 A/g.  The excellent electrochem. performance can be attributed to the introduction of the carbon-based materials, which not only serve as efficient buffering matrixes to tolerate the vol. changes of NiS upon sodiation/desodiation but also improve the electrode cond.  More importantly, this work provides a straightforward and general synthetic approach for designing various NiS/C composites as high-performance anodes for electrochem. energy storage.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Iron sulfide embedded porous carbon hollow spheres (FeSx@CS) have been successfully synthesized through a facile and environment friendly in situ synthetic method.  Using this method, a porous carbon matrix and uniformly dispersed iron sulfide nanoparticles were formed simultaneously and assembled into hollow spheres.  The as-synthesized FeSx@CS material showed high reversible capacity, outstanding cycle performance, and excellent rate capability when it was applied as an anode material for sodium ion batteries.  It delivered a remarkable charge capacity of 656.75 mA h g-1 in the first cycle and a capacity retention of 97% after 100 cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Three-dimensional (3D) carbon-wrapped iron sulfide interlocked graphene (Fe7S8@C-G) composites for high-performance sodium-ion storage are designed and produced through electrostatic interactions and subsequent sulfurization.  The iron-based metal-org. frameworks (MOFs, MIL-88-Fe) interact with graphene oxide sheets to form 3D networks, and carbon-wrapped iron sulfide (Fe7S8@C) nanoparticles with high individual-particle cond. are prepd. following a sulfurization process, surrounded by interlocked graphene sheets to enhance the interparticle cond.  The prepd. Fe7S8@C-G composites exhibit not only improved individual-particle and interparticle cond. to shorten electron/ion diffusion pathways, but also enhanced structural stability to prevent the aggregation of active materials and buffer large vol. changes during sodiation/desodiation.  As a sodium-ion storage material, the Fe7S8@C-G composites exhibit a reversible capacity of 449 mA h g-1 at 500 mA g-1 after 150 cycles and a retention capacity of 306 mA h g-1 under a c.d. of 2000 mA g-1.  The crucial factors related to the structural changes and stability during cycles have been further investigated.  These results demonstrate that the high-performance sodium-ion storage properties are mainly attributed to the uniquely designed three-dimensional configuration.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo9SozTLDs4l7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg8-uZq_0N9EA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXlt1OqtLk%253D&amp;md5=af53b3cee80eb2dacbc9a547ad4d48c0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref60" class="rightTabRefNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit60" data-doi="10.1039/D0TA04312E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ye, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, Z.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as promising low-cost alternatives to the prevailing lithium-ion batteries.  However, most anode materials for SIBs suffer from low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE), limiting their com. applications.  Herein, a Na-ion anode is demonstrated with an extremely high ICE of 99.4%, based on cobalt sulfide (Co9S8/CoS) nanoflakes grown on graphite foam (GF) in a diglyme-based electrolyte.  The achievement of such a high ICE can be ascribed to the following three aspects: negligible side reactions between a diglyme-based electrolyte and Co9S8/CoS, owing to a much higher Fermi level of diglyme redn. than anode potential μA of Co9S8/CoS, which can block electron transfer from the anode to the electrolyte, highly reversible conversion reaction of Co9S8/CoS, and much lower initial capacity loss of substrate GF, compared with other sorts of carbon.  The underlying rules revealed in this study serve as general guidelines in the development of sodium-ion anodes to achieve superb ICE.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqd4s-6DT9VKLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg8-uZq_0N9EA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXht1ejsLzO&amp;md5=028a31dc47e6654a4a30637d2e3e0386</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref61" class="rightTabRefNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit61" data-doi="10.1002/bbpc.19340400811"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zintl, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Harder, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dauth, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Gitterstruktur der Oxyde, Sulfide, Selenide und Telluride des Lithiums, Natriums und Kaliums</span>. <i>Z.     </div><div class="casAbstract">In layered type intercalation electrodes ions are stored reversibly during the functioning of secondary batteries.  The behavior of the system depends on geometrical and electronic factors.  The geometrical factors are concerned with the localization of the ions in the host structure; they deal with av. structure detns. and local ordering problems.  The diffusion properties of the intercalated ions depend on the site geometry, the population of the van der Waals gap, the ionicity of the bonds in the host, the stoichiometry of the host, and the mech. properties of its slabs.  Electrons have to be accommodated by the host.  The band structure of the host plays an important role in the ability to intercalate, the phase limit, and the stability of the products.  Metal-insulator transition may be induced.  Other possible factors such as Jahn-Teller effects have also to be considered.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGobDHSjnp7X1rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liMZxhXuSXapw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaL3sXktVyhu7s%253D&amp;md5=bc66e760e2a5ab23acb5fb0755047f66</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref66"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref66" class="rightTabRefNumLink">66</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit66" data-doi="10.1107/S0567739476001551"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shannon, R.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">20</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">28</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19129-19141</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report on results of a comprehensive investigation on reaction mechanisms occurring during Li uptake and release of the composite NiFe2O4/CNT.  Operando X-ray diffraction (XRD) and X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) data collected simultaneously using one in situ cell allowed thorough elucidation of structural and electronic alterations happening during Li uptake.  From the beginning of Li uptake, the Bragg intensity of the spinel reflections decreases which can be explained by redn. of Fe3+ ions and simultaneous movement of the Fe2+ cations from tetrahedral 8a to empty octahedral 16c sites.  The redn. of Fe3+ is clearly evidenced by XAS.  The occupation of tetrahedral sites by Li+ can be excluded based on results of d. functional theory calcns.  Increasing the Li content leads to formation of a new cryst. phase resembling a monoxide with a NaCl-like structure.  The appearance of the new phase is accompanied by a steady decrease of the sizes of coherently scattering domains of the spinel and a growth of the domains of the monoxide phase.  After uptake of about 2.5 Li per NiFe2O4, all Fe3+ cations are reduced to Fe2+ and the tetrahedral 8a sites are empty (XAS spectra).  Careful Rietveld refinements of X-ray powder patterns demonstrate that the tetrahedral 8a site is successively depleted with increasing Li content.  Interestingly, the occupancy of the octahedral 16d site is also slightly reduced.  Increasing the Li content beyond 2.5 Li/NiFe2O4 leads to successive redn. of the cations to very small metal particles embedded in a Li2O matrix (as evidenced by 7Li MAS NMR investigations).  During Li release metallic Ni and Fe are reoxidized to Ni2+ resp.  Fe3+.  The cycling stability of NiFe2O4/CNT is significantly improved compared to pure NiFe2O4 or a mech. mixt. of NiFe2O4 and CNTs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrLagi4gPqvKbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg8S9mDWealhw"> &gt;&gt;                         rightTab-cas_cit73R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">73</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Unveiling the Reaction Mechanism during Li Uptake and Release of Nanosized "NiFeMnO4": Operando X-ray Absorption, X-ray Diffraction, and Pair Distribution Function Investigations</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Permien, Stefan; Hansen, Anna-Lena; van Dinter, Jonas; Indris, Sylvio; Neubueser, Gero; Kienle, Lorenz; Doyle, Stephen; Mangold, Stefan; Bensch, Wolfgang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Omega</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2398-2409</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Here we report that the trimetallic nanosized oxide NiFeMnO4 consists of a mixt. of NiO and a strained cubic spinel phase, which is clearly demonstrated by anal. of the pair distribution function (PDF) and synchrotron x-ray data.  Such a finding can easily be overlooked by using only inhouse x-ray powder diffraction, leading to inaccurate assumption of the stoichiometry and oxidn. states.  Such advanced characterization is essential because a homogeneous distribution of the elements is obsd. in energy dispersive x-ray spectroscopy maps giving no hints of phase sepn.  Cycling of the sample against Li delivers a high reversible capacity of ≈ 840 mAh/g in the 50th cycle.  Operando x-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) expts. indicate that ≈ 0.8 Li/formula unit (f.u.) is consumed without detectable changes of the electronic structure.  Increasing the amt. of Li, Mn3+ and Fe3+ are simultaneously reduced.  The disappearance of the pre-edge features in the XANES indicates movement of these cations from tetrahedral sites to octahedral sites.  PDF anal. of the pattern after uptake of 2 Li/f.u. evidences that the principal structure can be sufficiently well modelled assuming coexisting NiO, a mixed monoxide and a small amt. of residual spinel phase.  Thus, the majority of cations are located on octahedral sites.  Furthermore, improvement of the PDF model is achieved taking into account small amts. of LiOH.  The 7Li MAS NMR spectrum of this sample clearly shows the signal of Li in a diamagnetic environment, excluding Li-O-TM bonds.  A further increase of the Li content leads to a successive conversion of the cations to nanosized metal particles embedded in a LiOH/Li2O matrix.  Ex situ XAS results indicate that Fe can be reversibly reoxidized to Fe3+ during charging, while Mn does not reach the oxidn. state obsd. in the pristine material.  After excessive cycling reoxidn. of metallic Ni is suppressed and contributes to the capacity loss compared to the early discharge/charge cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrrto-6ZdYrl7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg8S9mDWealhw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitVCqtr4%253D&amp;md5=a8292af42d11cf1435fc4c724c9ba270</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref74"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref74" class="rightTabRefNumLink">74</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit74" data-doi="10.1016/j.jssc.2008.06.034"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grosvenor, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopic (XPS) and x-ray absorption near-edge spectroscopic (XANES) measurements were made for several series of metal arsenide phosphides MAs1- yPy (M=Co, Fe, Cr) that adopt the MnP-type structure.  The P and As XPS binding energies (BEs) and XANES absorption energies of the metal arsenide phosphides do not follow the trend obsd. for the simple binary phosphides or arsenides, a deviation that arises from the combination of nearest and next-nearest neighbor contributions acting on the P or As photoemission or absorption site.  The P 2p 3/2 BEs and K-edge absorption energies are lower in MAs1- yPy than in MP because the P atoms are more neg. charged and because the P photoemission or absorption site is screened to a greater extent by less pos. charged nearest-neighbor M atoms and more neg. charged next-nearest neighbor P atoms.  The As L3- and K-edge absorption energies are higher in MAs1- yPy than in MAs primarily because the As atoms are less neg. charged.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The binary transition-metal monophosphides CrP, MnP, FeP, and CoP were studied with XPS.  The shifts in P 2p3/2 core line binding energies relative to that of elemental P indicated that the degree of ionicity of the metal-P bond decreases on progressing from CrP to CoP.  The metal 2p3/2 core line binding energies differ only slightly and show similar line shapes to those of the elemental metals, reaffirming the notion that these transition-metal phosphides have considerable metallic character.  The satellite structure obsd. in the Co 2p3/2 x-ray photoelectron spectra of Co metal and CoP was examd. by reflection EELS and was attributed to plasmon loss, not final state effects as was previously suggested.  Valence-band spectra of the transition-metal phosphides agree well with the d. of states profiles detd. from band structure calcns.  The electron populations of the different electronic states were extd. from the fitted valence-band spectra, and these confirm the presence of strong M-P and weak P-P bonding interactions.                         rightTab-cas_cit78R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">78</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Solid-state sodium-23 and aluminum-27 MAS NMR study of the dehydration of sodium aluminate hydrate (Na2O·Al2O3·3H2O)</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dec, Steven F.; Maciel, Gary E.; Fitzgerald, John J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">The dehydration of Na2O.Al2O3.3H2O was investigated as a function of dehydration temp. (100-600°) and dehydration time, by using solid-state 23Na and 27Al NMR with high-speed magic-angle spinning (MAS).  23Na and 27Al/MAS/NMR spectra were recorded at field strengths of 8.4, 11.7, and 14.0 T.  The field dependence of the NMR measurements aid in detg. the presence of a no. of chem. distinct sodium and aluminum sites.  The MAS NMR results indicate that both untreated and dehydrated Na2O.Al2O3.3H2O contain three chem. distinct sodium sites and one aluminum site.  Simulation of the obsd. 23Na and 27Al spectra yield quadrupole coupling consts. and asymmetry parameters for each sodium and aluminum site which, when used in combination with isotropic chem. shift measurements, permit various structures to be assigned to the sodium and aluminum sites.  Water wt.-loss measurements indicate that all structural changes obsd. in both the 23Na and 27Al/NMR spectra are related to the progress of dehydration, although not all the water is removed, even after heating at 598° for extended times.  For untreated Na2O.Al2O3.3H2O, the two major types of sodium sites are assigned to pseudo-octahedral structures, while the aluminum site is assigned to a four-coordinate aluminum with the local chem. environment, AlO2(OAl)2, i.e., with two coordinating oxygen atoms that are bonded to other aluminum atoms and two that are not.  For dehydrated Na2O.Al2O3.3H2O, the major sodium site is assigned to a pseudo-trigonal-bipyramidal structure, while the aluminum site is assigned to the tetrahedral AlO4 environment.  Minor sodium ion components in both hydrated and dehydrated Na2O.Al2O33H2O may exist in zones of irregular, partially dehydrated sites of the sodium oxide type.  A bulk chem. structural model of untreated Na2O.Al2O3.3H2O consistent with these structural assignments is one in which aluminum atoms are covalently linked together via oxygen bridges to form rings or polymer chains.  A bulk structural model of dehydrated Na2O.Al2O3.3H2O that is proposed is based on monomeric AlO4 tetrahedra linked by uniformly distributed five-coordinate sodium ions.  This bulk structure differs from that of the stable forms of dehydrated sodium aluminate, established by x-ray diffraction, in which both sodium and aluminum ions are tetrahedrally coordinated, indicating that NaAlO2 cannot be formed directly by dehydration of Na2O.Al2O3.3H2O with use of the dehydration temps. of this study.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrcbjE3E3BUd7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg8S9mDWealhw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK3cXmt1eiu7k%253D&amp;md5=6a9a3ebe621dee1ebc98a5c53adaeba9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref79"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref79" class="rightTabRefNumLink">79</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit79" data-doi="10.1246/cl.1984.293"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tabeta, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saito, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title"><sup>23</sup>Na Chemical Shifts of some Inorganic and Organic Compounds in the Solid State as Determined by the Magic Angle Spinning and High Power NMR Methods</span>. <i>Chem.                             rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref83"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref83" class="rightTabRefNumLink">83</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit83" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2018.12.070"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Morita, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gotoh, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tokiwa, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Arabnejad, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamashita, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deguchi, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ohki, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shimizu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Laskowski, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishida, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">States of Thermochemically or Electrochemically Synthesized Na<sub>x</sub>P<sub>y</sub> Compounds Analyzed by Solid State <sup>23</sup>Na and <sup>31</sup>P Nuclear Magnetic Resonance with Theoretical Calculation</span>. <i>J. Power Sources</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>413</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">418</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">424</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jpowsour.2018.12.070</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit83R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">83</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">States of thermochemically or electrochemically synthesized NaxPy compounds analyzed by solid state 23Na and 31P nuclear magnetic resonance with theoretical calculation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Morita, Ryohei; Gotoh, Kazuma; Dahbi, Mouad; Kubota, Kei; Komaba, Shinichi; Tokiwa, Kazuyasu; Arabnejad, Saeid; Yamashita, Koichi; Deguchi, Kenzo; Ohki, Shinobu; Shimizu, Tadashi; Laskowski, Robert; Ishida, Hiroyuki</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">413</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">418-424</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Phosphorus is a promising material for the electrode in sodium ion batteries (NIBs).  In this study, the states of NaxPy compds. synthesized by thermochem. reaction and electrochem. sodiation were compared using solid state 23Na magic angle spinning (MAS) NMR, 23Na multiple quantum (MQ) MAS NMR, and 31P MAS NMR.  The NMR signals in thermochem. synthesized NaxPy compds. (Na3P, NaP, Na3P7, Na3P11, NaP7) are assigned in ref. to theor. chem. shifts, based on first-principles calcns.  Furthermore, the NMR signals in electrochem. prepd. NaxPy compds. after two sodiation/desodiation cycles are ascribed to Na3P compds. and three amorphous compns.  The amorphous compds. ascribed to Na1-αP (0 &lt; α &lt; 1), Na2-βP (0 &lt; β &lt; 1), and Na3-γP (0 &lt; γ &lt; 1) are formed below 0.58 V in the charge and discharge process.  These Na3P and the amorphous phases overlapped in the range from 0.20 V (sodiation process) to 0.58 V (desodiation process). 23Na and 31P NMR spectra reveal reversible sodiation and desodiation processes in the third cycle.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpchCKH3MrCJ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhICs1c5UMdVQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXht1er&amp;md5=bcfcf92e0b9935181890488e20e20f39</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref84"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref84" class="rightTabRefNumLink">84</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit84" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.8b04183"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Marbella, L. E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Evans, M. L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Groh, M. F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nelson, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Griffith, K. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Morris, A. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grey, C. P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodiation and Desodiation via Helical Phosphorus Intermediates in High-Capacity Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. Am. Chem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>140</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">25</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">7994</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">8004</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/jacs.8b04183</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit84R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">84</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodiation and Desodiation via Helical Phosphorus Intermediates in High-Capacity Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Marbella, Lauren E.; Evans, Matthew L.; Groh, Matthias F.; Nelson, Joseph; Griffith, Kent J.; Morris, Andrew J.; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">140</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">25</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7994-8004</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are promising alternatives to Li-ion systems for electrochem. energy storage because of the higher natural abundance and widespread distribution of Na compared to Li.  High capacity anode materials, such as phosphorus, have been explored to realize Na-ion battery technologies that offer comparable performances to their Li-ion counterparts.  While P anodes provide unparalleled capacities, the mechanism of sodiation and desodiation is not well-understood, limiting further optimization.  Here, a combined exptl. and theor. approach are used to provide mol.-level insight into the (de)sodiation pathways in black P anodes for sodium-ion batteries.  A detn. of the P binding in these materials was achieved by comparing to structure models created via species swapping, ab initio random structure searching, and a genetic algorithm.  During sodiation, anal. of 31P chem. shift anisotropies in NMR data reveals P helixes and P at the end of chains as the primary structural components in amorphous NaxP phases.  X-ray diffraction data in conjunction with variable field 23Na magic-angle spinning NMR support the formation of a new Na3P crystal structure (predicted using d.-functional theory) on sodiation.  During desodiation, P helixes are re-formed in the amorphous intermediates, albeit with increased disorder, yet emphasizing the pervasive nature of this motif.  The pristine material is not re-formed at the end of desodiation and may be linked to the irreversibility obsd. in the Na-P system.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoS54XelnsLCbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhICs1c5UMdVQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhtFClt7nJ&amp;md5=f90d36c066e53e9b1df33743ec61de92</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i23">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge <i>in the online version of the paper</i>. Results of EDX measurements and Raman spectroscopy of pristine CrPS<sub>4</sub>; comparison of film vs powder electrodes; full view of XRD and PDF data of CrPS<sub>4</sub> electrodes at different discharge/charge state; <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR spectrum of NaCrS<sub>2</sub>. The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c14980?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c14980</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">(<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14980/suppl_file/am1c14980_si_001.pdf">am1c14980_si_001.pdf (1.14 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

	    }

	});

<script nonce="90f6e45c8ac5775c-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

g|\/(k|l|u)|50|54|\-[a-w])|libw|lynx|m1\-w|m3ga|m50\/|ma(te|ui|xo)|mc(01|21|ca)|m\-cr|me(rc|ri)|mi(o8|oa|ts)|mmef|mo(01|02|bi|de|do|t(\-| |o|v)|zz)|mt(50|p1|v )|mwbp|mywa|n10[0-2]|n20[2-3]|n30(0|2)|n50(0|2|5)|n7(0(0|1)|10)|ne((c|m)\-|on|tf|wf|wg|wt)|nok(6|i)|nzph|o2im|op(ti|wv)|oran|owg1|p800|pan(a|d|t)|pdxg|pg(13|\-([1-8]|c))|phil|pire|pl(ay|uc)|pn\-2|po(ck|rt|se)|prox|psio|pt\-g|qa\-a|qc(07|12|21|32|60|\-[2-7]|i\-)|qtek|r380|r600|raks|rim9|ro(ve|zo)|s55\/|sa(ge|ma|mm|ms|ny|va)|sc(01|h\-|oo|p\-)|sdk\/|se(c(\-|0|1)|47|mc|nd|ri)|sgh\-|shar|sie(\-|m)|sk\-0|sl(45|id)|sm(al|ar|b3|it|t5)|so(ft|ny)|sp(01|h\-|v\-|v )|sy(01|mb)|t2(18|50)|t6(00|10|18)|ta(gt|lk)|tcl\-|tdg\-|tel(i|m)|tim\-|t\-mo|to(pl|sh)|ts(70|m\-|m3|m5)|tx\-9|up(\.b|g1|si)|utst|v400|v750|veri|vi(rg|te)|vk(40|5[0-3]|\-v)|vm40|voda|vulc|vx(52|53|60|61|70|80|81|83|85|98)|w3c(\-| )|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>


                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="90f6e45c8ac5775c-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

    console.log("ACSPubs2 start");
    $.getJSON('https://extreme-ip-lookup.com/json/', function(data, status) {
            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");
                });

                    cookie=status;
            }

            cookie1 = cookie;
            cookie = cookie1 + "; path=/; domain=.acs.org";
            //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
